And if 14 ... ♕xg6 15 ♔h1 ♖g8 16 ♖g1, the g-file might have been more useful to him than to Black (17 ♗h3 ♕f7 18 ♖xg8+ ♕xg8 19 ♖g1). Instead, Black developed, 14 ... b6 15 ♔h1 ♗b7 and White began positional pressure, 16 ♘c1! ♕xg6 17 ♘d3 O-O-O 18 ♖ac1. Black's lack of play on the h-file was felt after 18 ... ♖hg8 19 ♖g1 ♕f6 20 b4 ♔b8 21 a3. When Black tried to force matters with 21 ... f4 22 ♘fe5 c5 he quickly collapsed, 23 bxc5 ♘xe5 24 dxe5 ♕f5 25 c6. It's important for the defender to realize he may have a choice of which lines to open: White's bishop ���doesn't play' and his rook is a bit misplaced at d4. But what matters most is hxg6. For example, 1 ... ♘f6 2 hxg6 hxg6 and now 3 ♕g5! (3 ... ♕xd4 4 ♕xg6+ ♖g7 5 ♕xg7+ ♔xg7 6 ♘xf5+ and ♘xd4). Black solved his problem with 1 ... g5!. If White allows the kingside to be closed (2 ♕d2 h6) Black will dominate the e-file and/or win the d5-pawn after ... ♖e5. So White accepted the pawn, 2 ♕xg5+ ♖g7 3 ♕e3. But after 3 ... f4! 4 ♕e6+ ♔h8 5 ♘e2 ♘e5 it became clear that Black's threats were much more powerful, e.g. 6... ♗c8! 7 ♕h6 ♘g4, trapping the queen. White played 6 ♖xf4 and resigned soon after 6 ... ♘d3+ 7 ♔b1 ♘xf4. Dead Pawn One of the best ways to neutralize a potentially explosive file is to create a 'dead pawn.' This is an advanced enemy pawn that is deliberately not captured because it will block a file. A.Geller - Spassky, Leningrad 1954
1 e4 c5 2 ♘f3 d6 3 d4 cxd4 4 ♘xd4 ♘f6 5 ♘c3 a6 6 ♗g5 e6 7 ♕f3 ♗e7 8 O-O-O ♕c7
9 g4 b5 10 ♖g1 b4 11 ♘ce2 ♗b7 12 ♗xf6 ♗xf6 13 g5 ♗xd4 14 ♘xd4 ♘d7 15 ♕e2
♘c5 16 ♗g2 O-O 17 ♔b1 ♖fd8 18 h4 a5 19 h5 e5 20 ♘f5 ♘e6 21 ♕g4 a4 22 g6 b3 On 22 h6? Black would have kept the kingside closed with 22 ... g6!. But after 22 g6! at least one attacking file will be partly open. However, White saw that there was no follow-up to 23 gxh7+. He should deal instead with Black's threats, beginning with 23 ... bxc2+. The most natural defense is 23 cxb3 axb3 24 axb3. But this loses to 24 ... ♖a1+! 25 ♔xa1 ♕c2 and ... ♖a8 (mate). Moreover 24 a3, instead of 24 axb3, also fails, to 24 ... ♕c2+ 25 ♔a1 ♖xa3+! 26 bxa3 ♕a2 mate. And 23 axb3 axb3 24 c3 loses after 24 ... ♕a5. White found 23 cxb3 axb3 24 ♖c1! and then 24 ... bxa2+ 25 ♔a1. The pawn at a2 is 'dead' and blocking Black's attack. There is a new target now, at b2, but it will take at least three moves for Black's heavy pieces to menace it. And 25 ... ♘c5, with a threat of ... ♘b3 mate, loses to 25 ♖xc5! followed by a killing capture on f7 or h7. Black met the threat to his queen with 25 ... ♕d7 and left White to choose a pawn capture on the kingside. He picked 26 gxf7+ since 26 ... ♕xf7?? loses to 27 ♘h6+. Also bad is 26 ... ♔h8 because of 27 ♘xg7! ♘xg7 28 f8(♕)+ ♖xf8 29 ♕xd7. So 26 ... ♔f8 was forced. There were two dead pawns now. White's attack looks like the faster one because he can move his bishop and threaten ♘xg7. Chances would be roughly equal after 27 ♗f1 ♕xf7 (and 28 ♗c4 d5, for example). But White decided to take aim at d6. After 27 ♖gd1? ♖dc8! and 28 ♖xc8+ ♕xc8! Black was regaining the initiative. For example, 29 ♘xd6 ♘c5! threatens ... ♘b3 mate and allows 30 ♕g3 ♕g4! and wins. The rest was one-sided, 29 ♕g3 ♕c2 30 ♖e1 ♔xf7 31 ♘xd6+ ♔g8 32 h6? ♕d2!
33 f4 exf4 34 ♕c3 ♕xd6 33 ♕b3 ♗c8 34 ♖d1 ♖b8 White resigns.
Repairing Pushing pawns around your castled king is likely to make it more vulnerable. But some pawn weaknesses can be masked, if not eliminated, with the help of a piece. Tal - Witkowski, Riga 1959
1 e4 e5 2 ♘f3 ♘c6 3 ♗b5 a6 4 ♗a4 ♘f6 5 O-O ♗e7 6 ♖e1 b5 7 ♗b3 O-O 8 c3 d5 9
exd5 ♘xd5 10 ♘xe5 ♘xe5 11 ♖xe5 ♘f6 12 d4 ♗d6 13 ♖e2 ♘h5 14 ♕d3 ♕h4 15 g3 ♕h3 Black's adoption of the old, original line of the Marshall Gambit appears to be working well, thanks to the weakness he provoked, g2-g3. He is preparing ... ♗g4, ... ♗f5 or ... ♗b7, followed by ... ♖ae8. It's natural to try to catch up in development. But 16 ♘d2? loses to 16 ... ♘f4!. And 16 ♗e3 walks into 16 ... ♗f5 (17 ♕d1 ♗g4 or 17 ♕d2 ♗e4 18 f4 ♘xg3). Instead, White repaired his only weakness with 16 ♗d5! followed by ♗g2. This turned his kingside into a solid fianchetto setup. Black tried 16 ... ♗f5. But after 17 ♕e3! he couldn't challenge the e-file because 17 ... ♖ae8? 18 ♕xe8! favors White. Instead, he chose 17 ... ♖ad8 and White had eliminated virtually all kingside danger with 18 ♗g2! ♕g4 19 ♘d2 ♕g6 20 ♘f3. Trading We're not done with that last example. After White had repaired his kingside with shifts of minor pieces, Black continued 20 ... ♗g4. Black is preparing ... f5 followed by ... f4 or ... ♖de8. He may even find an opportunity when the sacrifice ... ♘f4 works. But he didn't get the chance because White forced queens off the board with 21 ♕g5!. The attack that was fatally wounded by ♗d5-g2 was buried after 21 ... ♖de8 22 ♕xg6 hxg6 23 ♖xe8 ♖xe8 24 ♗e3. Black, still a pawn down, tried 24 ... f5? but his kingside pieces were at risk after 25 ♘e5 ♗xe5 26 dxe5 threatened f2-f3. He resigned soon after 26 ... f4 27 gxf4. Transition into an ending is a powerful resource because it exploits an imbalance that frequently emerges from an opening: One player concedes positional assets - such as a superior pawn structure or the two-bishop edge - in return for an initiative. He is offering a kind of non- material gambit. But if his opponent can swap down to an ending, what is left are those positional assets. Kharlov - Dreev, St. Petersburg 1993
1 e4 e6 2 d4 d5 3 ♘c3 ♘f6 4 ♗g5 dxe4 5 ♘xe4 ♗e7 6 ♗xf6 ♗xf6 7 ♘f3 O-O 8 ♕d2
b6 9 O-O-O ♗b7 10 ♕f4 ♗e7 11 ♗d3 ♘d7 12 h4 White is ready to put a knight on g5 and, if ... ♗xg5, retake with the h-pawn and obtain a powerful attack on the h-file. For example, 12 ... ♘f6 13 ♘xf6+ ♗xf6 14 ♘g5 ♗xg5? 15 hxg5 (15 ... g6 16 ♖h6 and 17 ♖dh1). Black lacks the usual center play because 12 ... c5 13 dxc5 poses problems on the d-file (13 ... bxc5 14 ♗b5; 13 ... ♘xc5 14 ♘xc5 bxc5 15 ♗xh7+ and ♖xd8). But Black has a nice resource, 12 ... ♕b8!. Then 13... c5 will not only attack the center but offer a queen trade that would favor his two bishops. White tried 13 ♘e5 but 13 ... c5! undermined the knight since 14 ♘xd7?? would hang the queen (14 ... ♕xf4+). White protected it with 14 g3?, rather than be more consistent with 14 ♘g5!. There followed 14 ... ♘xe5 15 dxe5. White seems to be ready for 16 ♘g5!? or g3-g4-g5 and ♘f6+!. But Black upset him with 15 ... f5!. White had to choose between a move of the pinned knight, which costs the Exchange, or the endgame of 16 exf6 ♕xf4+!. He chose 16 ♘g5? but after 16 ... ♗xh1 17 ♖xh1 ♕b7 and 18 f3 ♗xg5 19 hxg5 g6 he had no attack and little compensation. He lost quickly. Neutralizing You already know that it takes several pieces to mount a meaningful attack. If the defender can neutralize even one dangerous piece, he may reduce the chances of an initiative drastically. A.Sokolov - Karpov, Candidates match 1987
1 e4 c6 2 d4 d5 3 ♘d2 dxe4 4 ♘xe4 ♘d7 5 ♗c4 ♘gf6 6 ♘g5 e6 7 ♕e2 ♘b6 8 ♗d3
h6 9 ♘5f3 c5 10 dxc5 ♗xc5 11 ♘e5 ♘bd7 12 ♘gf3 ♕c7 13 O-O O-O 14 ♗d2 ♗d6 15 ♘xd7 ♗xd7 16 ♖ae1 ♖fd8 17 ♘e5 White would have preferred to keep his other knight on the board. But he didn't like the looks of 15 ♖fe1 ♘c5! because ... ♘xd3! would have eliminated a very dangerous bishop. But now he is ready for kingside action with 18 f4 or 18 g3 followed by ♘g4. If Black trades queens, 17 ... ♗xe5 18 ♕xe5 ♕xe5 19 ♖xe5, White's two bishops and queenside majority favor him considerably. But Black killed the attack with 17 ... ♗b5! and the idea of 18 ... ♗xd3 (or 18 ... ♗xe5 19 ♕xe5 ♕xe5 20 ♖xe5 and then 20 ... ♗xd3). The game went 18 ♗xb5 ♗xe5 19 ♕xe5 ♕xe5 20 ♖xe5 ♖xd2. White's bishop-over-knight advantage is not nearly as strong as the two-bishop edge would have been. He temporarily trapped the advanced rook with 21 ♗d3. But with aggressive play, 21 ... ♖c8 22 ♖ee1 b5! and 23 a3 ♘d5 24 ♖d1 ♖xd1 25 ♖xd1 a5 26 g3 b4! 27 axb4 ♘xb4, Black was able to trade into a drawish rook endgame. It is particularly important to swap off an opponent's piece if it is vital to his defense as well as to his attack. White has potentially vulnerable pawns at b2, d4, f3 and h2 and a possibly mateable king. Black's last move, ... ♘(f6)-h5, prepared ... ♕f6 as well as ... ♘f4/... ♖h6/... ♕c8-h3. This position had arisen before, when 1 ♔h1 was played. Then 1 ... ♘f4 could be met by 2 ♖g1, safeguarding g2. But Black still had pressure against h2 after 2 ... ♖h6! and 3 ♘d5 ♘xd5 4 ♗xd5 ♕f6 followed by ... g6!, ... ♔g7 and ... ♖bh8. White's improvement was 1 ♘d5!. It stops ... ♕f6 and allows him to trade off the dangerous Black knight after it lands on f4. Black's chances for initiative vanished after 1 ... ♘f4 2 ♕d2! and 2 ... ♘xd5 3 ♗xd5. Moreover White was ready to grab the initiative after the game went 3 ... ♖h6 4 ♖fa1!. With ♖a8! coming up, Black's king is suddenly at greater risk than White's. For example, 4 ... ♕f6 5 ♖a8! ♖xa8 6 ♖xa8+ ♔h7 and now 7 e5! dxe5 8 ♕c2+! g6 9 ♖f8 and ♖xf7+ wins. Note how the splendid bishop defends f3 and also attacks f7. Black tried 4 ... ♕d7. But again 5 ♖a8! ♖xa8 6 ♖xa8+ ♔h7 made 7 e5! strong. If Black had gone after the h2-pawn, 7 ... ♕h3, he would lose to 8 ♗xf7 ♕xh2+ 9 ♔f1 ♕h3+ 10 ♔e2 (10 ... g4 11 ♕d3+ g6 12 e6!). Instead, Black played 7 ... dxe5 8 dxe5 ♕h3. But this time 9 ♗xf7 had a threat of 10 ♖h8+! ♔xh8 11 ♕d8+ and mates, which proved decisive. Trading isn't the only way to neutralize an annoying piece. Lenier Dominguez - Short, Havana 2010
1 d4 e6 2 c4 ♘f6 3 g3 c5 4 ♘f3 cxd4 5 ♘xd4 ♕c7 6 ♘c3 a6 7 ♗g2 ♕xc4 8 O-O ♘c6
9 ♘b3 d5 10 ♗f4 ♗e7 11 a3 d4 12 ♘d2 ♕c5 13 ♘ce4 ♘xe4 14 ♘xe4 ♕b6 15 ♘d6+ ♗xd6 16 ♗xd6 White's minor pieces, particularly the bishop on d6, are clearly superior to Black's. Black seems to have only unpleasant options such as 16 ... ♗d7 17 b4 O-O- 0, which allows 18 ♕d2 followed by ♖fb1/ a3-a4 and b4-b5. Or 16 ... ♕xb2 17 ♖b1 ♕c3 18 ♕a4. But Black found the 'ugly' solution to his problems, 16 ... ♔d7!. He would meet 17 ♗xc6+ with 17 ... ♔xd6!. Then he can keep his extra pawn and bring his king to safety (... ♔e7, ... ♖d8). White managed to equalize after 17 ♗f4 ♔e7 18 ♗xc6 bxc6 19 ♗e5 c5 20 b4!. But 17 ... f6! and ... e5 would have rendered the once powerful bishop harmless and given Black winning chances. Contesting The pawn structure that evolves from the opening often dictates the wing that a player will have more operating room. A common error is to write off the other flank as belonging to your opponent. Karjakin - Carlsen, Wijk aan Zee 2010
1 e4 e6 2 d4 d5 3 ♘c3 ♘f6 4 e5 ♘fd7 5 f4 c5 6 ♘f3 ♘c6 7 ♗e3 ♗e7 8 ♕d2 O-O 9
♗e2 a6 10 O-O b5 11 ♔h1 ♕c7 12 a3 ♗b7 13 ♖ad1 ♖ac8 14 ♕e1 cxd4 15 ♘xd4 ♘xd4 16 ♗xd4 ♗c5 17 ♕h4 "When you have a pawn on e5 you must attack" goes the maxim. White moved his queen to h4 to target h7 with ♗d3 or ♖f3-h3. Should Black try to distract him with counterplay on the queenside, his wing? No, the right idea was 17 ... ♗xd4 18 ♖xd4 f6!. White replied 19 ♗d3 h6. Then 20 ♕g4 f5 would close the d3-h7 diagonal. White felt forced to liquidate with 20 exf6 ♖xf6. Black has turned the kingside from a liability into an asset. His bad bishop isn't as important as the target f4-pawn. White would be the one forced to defend after 21 ♖g1 ♖cf8 22 ♘e2 e5! 23 fxe5 ♘xe5, for example. No better is 21 g3, which may open the long diagonal for Black's bishop after 21 ... e5. White tried 21 f5 ♖cf8 22 ♖g1 instead. But he was distinctly worse after 22 ... ♘c5! 23 fxe6 ♘xe6 24 ♖g4?! ♘f4 25 ♕g3 ♕e7. To stop ... d4 and an attack on g2, he gave up the Exchange, 26 ♖xf4 ♖xf4, but it wasn't nearly enough. Relieving When we talk of an opponent's pressure, we often mean a pin on a file or diagonal. Breaking a pin can incur great cost: When a White bishop on g5 pins a Black knight at f6 it can be highly weakening to drive the bishop back with ... h6 and ... g5. Computers have taught us that many pins can be safely broken that way. But following Steinitz's principle of economy, it is often better to relieve the pin by either tactics or maneuver. Here's a tactical example. Spassky - Larsen, Belgrade 1970
1 d4 ♘f6 2 c4 e6 3 ♘f3 b6 4 ♘c3 ♗b7 5 ♗g5 ♗e7 6 e3 O-O 7 ♗d3 c5 8 O-O ♘c6 9
d5 ♘b4 10 d6 ♗xd6 11 ♗xh7+ ♔xh7 12 ♕xd6 ♗xf3 13 gxf3 Because Black's h-pawn is gone, he can't relieve the pin with ... h6. And if he doesn't do something fast he will lose the d7-pawn or be forced into passivity after 14 ♖ad1. But Black had counted on 13 ... ♘e8!. The first point is that on a retreat of the attacked White queen, to g3 for example, Black can safely embarrass the bishop with 14 ... f6. More complex are the two Exchange sacrifices that Black offered: (a) He would stand well after 14 ♗xd8 ♘xd6 15 ♗e7 ♘xc4 and 16 ♗xf8 ♖xf8. (b) Better is 14 ♕xf8! ♕xg5+ 15 ♔h1. Black thought he would have sufficient compensation after 15 ... ♕h5 16 ♖g1 ♕xf3+ 17 ♖g2 g6. (Actually, 18 ♔g1! favors White a bit.) Instead he got a tiny edge with 14 ♕e7 ♕xe7 15 ♗xe7 ♖g8 but lost. A creative way to break a pin by a maneuver is illustrated by this:
1 e4 e5 2 ♘f3 ♘c6 3 ♘c3 ♘f6 4 ♗b5 ♗b4 5 O-O O-O 6 d3 d6 7 ♗g5 ♗xc3 8 bxc3
♕e7 9 ♖e1 White protected his e-pawn with the rook so that he can exert pressure on the center with 10 d4. Black should avoid 10 ... exd4 11 cxd4, since White's edge in the center would mean he threatens 12 e5. A surprising plan discovered a century ago, known as the Metger Unpin, proves effective. It begins with 9 ... ♘d8! and then 10 d4 ♘e6. Black wants to play ... ♘xg5. If White retreats the bishop to d2 or c1, Black can apply his own pressure to the center with ... c5. (This is based on a small trap, 11 ♗c1 c5 2 dxe5 dxe5 13 ♘xe5? ♘c7! and Black wins one of the attacked minor pieces.) But what happens if White maintains the pin with 11 ♗h4 ? The answer is 11 ... ♘f4! followed by 12 ... ♘g6 13 ♗g5 h6!. True, Black spends four tempi with his knight. But it is worth it to break the pin that was the key to White's early middlegame pressure. Confusing By 'confusing' we don't mean complicating the position to such a degree that it bewilders an opponent. No, confusing is upsetting the harmonious interplay of enemy pieces. When enemy pieces are coordinated they become dangerous. To stop that, they should be driven to bad squares and their protection from other pieces should be cut. W.Adams - Reshevsky, New York 1936
1 e4 e6 2 d4 d5 3 ♘c3 ♗b4 4 ♗d3 dxe4 5 ♗xe4 ♘f6 6 ♗g5 h6 7 ♗xf6 ♕xf6 8 ♘f3
O-O 9 O-O ♘d7 10 ♘e2 ♗d6 11 c4 c5 12 ♖c1 cxd4 13 ♘exd4 a6 14 a3 ♘c5 15 ♗b1 White's knights are nicely centralized and he is ready to seize space with 16 b4 and 17 c5. To thwart that Black needs to push White's pieces around. He started with 15 ... ♗f4! and then 16 ♖c3 ♖d8!. Black's pin on the d-file means he threatens 17 ... e5, e.g. 17 b4 e5! 18 bxc5 exd4 19 ♖d3 ♗e5 (20 ♖e1 ♗g4!). White found 17 ♕c2, feeling that 17 ... ♖xd4 18 ♘xd4 ♕xd4 19 ♖d1 and ♕h7+ was good for him. Black agreed and play went 17 ... g6 18 b4: A key to successful confusing is not to give your opponent time to regroup. Black shot back 18 ... e5! since 19 ♘e2 ♗f5! would have made his advantage obvious. White went for 19 bxc5 exd4 20 ♖b3 instead. Then 20 ... ♗d7! threatened 21 ... ♗a4 and invited 21 ♖xb7 ♗c6. White preferred 21 ♕b2 but Black kept hitting his pieces and pawns, 21 ... ♗c6 22 ♖d1 ♕e7 23 ♖bd3 ♕xc5 24 ♘xd4 ♗e5!. After a liquidation, 25 ♕d2 ♗xd4! 26 ♖xd4 ♖xd4 27 ♕xd4, he won with 27 ... ♕xa3 28 ♕f6 ♕b3! 29 ♖e1 ♖e8! 30 ♖f1 ♕xc4. Traditional defense said enemy pieces should be restrained. New Defense said they should be confused, usually by threatening them or other pieces. The most forceful threat is, of course, a check. Euwe - Reshevsky, AVRO 1938
1 d4 ♘f6 2 c4 g6 3 f3 d5 4 cxd5 ♘xd5 5 e4 ♘b6 6 ♘c3 ♗g7 7 ♗e3 O-O 8 f4 ♘c6 9
d5 ♘b8 10 ♘f3 c6 11 ♕b3 cxd5 12 ♘xd5 ♘xd5 13 exd5 ♘d7 14 ♗e2 After 13 exd5 White's d-pawn can become weak or strong, depending on whether he can coordinate his pieces and defend it (O-O and ♖fd1). But 14 ... ♕a5+! offered White a choice of which of three pieces to misplace. He decided against 15 ♘d2 ♘b6 because he can't defend the pawn with ♖d1. And he rejected 15 ♔f2 because 15 ... ♘f6 would threaten the d-pawn as well as ... ♘g4+. That left 15 ♗d2. Black replied 15 ... ♕b6! since a trade of queens (16 ♕xb6 ♘xb6) would cost the d-pawn. White tried 16 ♗c3 ♗xc3+ 17 bxc3. But then 17 ... ♕e3! ruined White's plans. He would be losing after 18 g3 ♘c5 19 ♕c4 ♘e4 or 19 ♕c2 ♗f5. Instead, he gave up a pawn, 18 c4 ♕xf4 19 O-O. But his hopes of kingside attack were squelched by a piece trade, 19 ... ♕c7 20 ♔h1 ♘f6 21 ♕e3 ♗g4 22 ♕h6 ♗xf3!. Black won following 23 ♖xf3 b5 24 cxb5 ♕e5 25 ♖e1 ♘xd5. Reinforcing Successful attacks are based on a local superiority in firepower: Two attacking pieces are likely to beat one defender. Three attackers will usually outmuscle two defenders, and so on. The simplest way to change an unfavorable imbalance is to add one or more defenders: White's last move, g2-g4, guarantees that he will get back his sacrificed piece with g4-g5. But he's also aiming for mate after f4-f5 followed by f5-f6 or ♗xh6. His queen and rook, with the potential addition of his knight and bishop, might overwhelm the kingside. But Black's 1 ... ♕d8! rushed his queen back in time to defend f6 and g5 or offer a trade (after ... f6, ... ♕e8). And while 2 g5 f6! 3 gxh6 would get White's piece back, 3 ... g6! would close key lines thanks to the dead h-pawn. White would be worse. Another idea is 2 f5 and ♗xh6. But 2 ... ♔g8 3 ♗xh6 g6! would trap the queen (4 fxg6 fxg6+). White chose 2 ♘g1 so that his knight can emerge at f3. But after 2 ... f6 3 f5 ♕e8! a queen trade was coming. White was losing after 4 ♕xe8 ♖xe8 5 exf6 ♗xf6 and resigned after 6 g5 ♖xf5+ 7 ♘f3 ♗d8 8 gxh6 g5! 9 ♖g3 g4 10 ♖xg4 ♖xf3+ 11 ♔e2 ♖ef8. Reinforcement pieces don't nominate themselves. The defender may need to re- examine each of his pieces to find the right one. Black is taking aim at g3 and preparing to attack h2. Until White deals with that he can't safely harvest pawns on the queenside. For example, after 1 ♕xa7? ♕h5 Black has a powerful threat of 2 ... ♖h6 and 3 ... ♕xh2 mate. Another illustration of the force of her pieces is 1 f4 ♕xg3+! with perpetual check (2 hxg3 ♖xg3+ 3 ♔h1 ♖h3+). But White can defend his kingside if he realizes he has a rook doing nothing on the queenside. With 1 ♖a2! he anticipated 1 ... ♕f6 2 f3! ♘g5 3 f4, for example. He even has a threat, 2 f4! (since ... ♕xg3+ no longer works). Black freed her queen from defending the rook at d8 with 1 ... ♖f8 and that permitted her to meet 2 f4 with 2 ... ♕g4. The point is that 3 fxe5 allows 3 ... ♘xg3! (4 ♖g2? ♘e2+!). However, White secured his king's safety with 3 ♕e7 ♖g8 4 ♖g2!. Now g3 and h2 are rock solid and White is free to cash the queenside. After 4 ... ♘d3 5 ♕xa7 Black saw that sacrifices on g3 were doomed to fail. For example, 5 ... ♕h3 6 ♕a6! ♘xg3 7 ♕xd3! wins for White. The same for 6 ... ♘xc1 7 ♖xc1 ♘xg3 8 hxg3 ♖xg3 9 ♖xg3 ♕xg3+ 10 ♔f1 ♕xe3 11 ♘e2. Black had one remaining attacking idea, ... h5-h4xg3. But thanks to that rook at g2 White defended easily, 5 ... h5 6 ♕a6! ♘xc1 7 ♖xc1 h4 8 ♕e2!, and forced a trade of queens into a won endgame. Striking The defender often finds himself with a limited presence in the center. Advancing a pawn to the fourth rank is a way of (a) countering a wing attack, or (b) establishing a foothold for later counterplay. Botvinnik - Ragozin, training match 1936
1 e4 c5 2 ♘f3 e6 3 d4 cxd4 4 ♘xd4 ♘f6 5 ♘c3 d6 6 ♗e2 a6 7 ♗e3 ♕c7 8 a4 b6 9 f4
♗b7 10 ♗f3 ♘bd7 11 ♕e2 ♗e7 12 O-O O-O 13 g4 Black faces 14 g5 and an attack on h7 after ♗g2/♖f3-h3/♕h5. He could have played 13 ... h6. But this violates the principle of economy and makes 14 h4/15 g5 stronger. Steinitz might have played 13 ... ♖fd8 so he could defend with ... ♘f8. But Black wanted counterplay with 13 ... d5! 14 e5 ♘e4! instead. Then 15 ♗xe4 dxe4 16 g5 enables Black to fight for the initiative after 16 ... ♖fd8 and ... ♖ac8/... ♗b4. White preferred 15 ♘xe4 dxe4 16 ♗g2. Even though Black may lose the pawn at e4 he has favorably changed the nature of the middlegame. Play went 16 ... ♗d5 17 ♖fc1 ♖ac8 - so that 18 ♕xa6 ♗c4 19 ♘b5 ♕c6 would threaten 20 ... ♖a8. White went after the e-pawn instead with 18 ♗f2 ♘c5 19 b4. Black unleashed his defensive power with 19 ... ♘d3! 20 cxd3 ♕xc1+ 21 ♖xc1 ♖xc1+. He would overwhelm White after 22 ♗e1 ♗h4 or, as the game went, with 22 ♗f1 ♖fc8 followed by ... ♖a1 and ... ♖cc1. Another example: Suttles - Fischer, Palma de Mallorca 1970
1 e4 ♘f6 2 e5 ♘d5 3 d4 d6 4 c4 ♘b6 5 exd6 cxd6 6 ♗e3 g6 7 d5 ♗g7 8 ♗d4 ♗xd4 9
♕xd4 O-O 10 ♘c3 There are no Black kingside pieces to counter a plan such as h2-h4-h5xg6. Bringing in reinforcements (... ♘8d7-f6) would leave Black on the defensive for some time. The correct defense is 10 ... e5!. Black would have too much play after 11 dxe6 ♗xe6 and 12 ... ♘c6. So White let the new center stand with 11 ♕d2 f5! 12 ♘f3 ♘8d7 13 O-O-O ♕f6. Black had the better prospects following 14 ♕h6 ♕e7 15 ♖e1 e4! 16 ♘d2 ♘e5 17 h3 ♘bd7 and his superiority was obvious after 18 ♕e3 ♕h4 19 g3 ♕f6 20 ♔b1 ♘c5 21 f4? exf3 22 ♘xf3 f4! 23 gxf4 ♘xf3 24 ♕xf3 ♕h4. Escaping When reinforcements are not readily available to an endangered wing, it may make sense just to flee. In many cases you don't have to run far. Bernstein - Fischer, Montreal 1956
1 d4 ♘f6 2 ♘f3 g6 3 ♘c3 d5 4 ♗f4 ♗g7 5 e3 O-O 6 ♗e2 ♘h5 7 ♗g5 h6 8 ♗h4 g5 9
♗g3 ♘xg3 10 hxg3 c5 11 ♕d3 e6 12 ♘e5 f5 13 g4 f4 14 O-O-O ♘c6 15 ♖xh6? ♗xh6 16 ♕g6+ ♗g7 17 ♗d3 White's threat of 18 ♕h7 mate can be parried by 17 ... ♘xe5. But after 18 dxe5 White threatens 19 ♖h1 followed by 20 ♖h8+! ♔xh8 21 ♕h7 mate. Easier seems 17 ... ♖f6 18 ♕xg5 fxe3 but White still has some play after 19 ♘xc6 bxc6 21 fxe3 followed by ♗g6/♕h5/g4-g5. Black opted for the simplest way out, 17 ... ♘xe5 18 dxe5 ♖f7 and 19 ♘b5 ♔f8!. Black seized the initiative after 20 ♘d6 ♖d7 21 ♗b5 ♖xd6 22 exd6 ♗d7 23 ♗xd7 ♕xd7. A more elaborate example of escape: Speelman - Luther, Lippstadt 2000 1 ♘f3 d5 2 c4 e6 3 d4 ♘f6 4 ♘c3 c6 5 e3 ♘bd7 6 ♕c2 ♗d6 7 ♗d3 O-O 8 O-O dxc4 9 ♗xc4 a6 10 ♗d3 c5 11 ♗d2 ♕c7 12 ♖ac1 c4 13 ♘e4 ♘xe4 14 ♗xe4 f5 15 ♗d3 b5 16 b3! ♗b7 17 bxc4 ♖f6 Black's last move prepares 18 ... ♖g6 and 18 ... ♗xf3 19 gxf3 ♗xh2+ (20 ♔g2 ♖g6+). What about reinforcements? The problem with 18 ♕d1 is that 18 ... ♖g6 already threatens 19 ... ♗xh2+ 20 ♘xh2? ♖xg2+. Rather than 18 cxb5 ♕b8 White preferred 18 ♖fe1!? because it creates an escape hatch for the king at f1. (The rook is better placed at e1 than d1 in case White gets to play 19 e4.) Black inflicted kingside damage, 18 ... ♗xf3 19 gxf3 ♖g6+ 20 ♔f1, and then played 20 ... ♗a3!, clearing a path for his queen to h2. Play continued 21 cxb5 ♕xh2. White would like to break the Black initiative with 22 ♕c7 and then 22 ... ♕h3+ 23 ♔e2 ♗xc1 24 ♖xc1 ♘f6 25 b6 and 26 b7. But Black has better in 22 ... ♗d6! 23 ♕xd7 ♖g2!. White's king is a fatal liability, e.g. 24 ♖e2 ♕h1 mate; 24 ♔e2 ♖xf2+ 25 ♔d1 ♖xd2 mate and 24 ♕xe6+ ♔h8 25 ♖c8+ ♗f8!. The right defense is escape: 22 ♔e2! since he can cover f2 with a rook or bishop (22 ... ♖g2 23 ♖f1). The game went 22 ... ♗xc1 23 ♖xc1 axb5 24 ♕c6 ♖a7? 25 ♗xf5! and wins (25 ... exf5 26 ♕d5+ and ♖c8+ or 25 ... ♖f6 26 ♗xe6+! ♔h8 27 ♕xd7!). And now let's see what you've learned about the weapons of defense. Quiz White can make progress with ♖af1. What can Black do? Should Black capture on h5, allow hxg6 or pick door number three? Should Black be worried about a sacrifice on h6? How does he best safeguard his king? White will have attacking chances if he can play ♖e3 and ♘h5. For example, 1 ... ♗a6 2 ♖e3 ♗xc4 and now 3 ♘h5 ♘xh5 leaves White with a choice between 4 ♕xh5 f5 5 ♗xg7! (or 5 ♖g3 g6 6 ♖xg6+) and 4 ♗xh7+! ♔xh7 5 ♕xh5+ ♔g8 6 ♗xg7! and wins. What can Black do instead? White's attack is based on h4-h5xg6 and ♖h4. Is 1 ... ♗g7 the best? White can try to penetrate on the h-file. But ♖g2, ♘f2 and g3-g4xf5 - or the immediate g3-g4 - are also attractive. What should Black do? Mikhail Tal could defend when he wanted to. Here he faces an ominous ♖h3. Does 1 ... g6 work? Is there anything better? White seems to have an edge in the center. What can Black do? Evaluate 1...fxg6 2 hxg6 h6 and 2...hxg6. Should Black just ignore the g6-pawn? Chapter Five: Counterplay "When you have Black, your goal is to equalize," masters used to say. "If you accomplish that, you can try for an advantage," they added. That was the credo of great players like Jose Capablanca, Akiba Rubinstein and Paul Keres. But they didn't play the Najdorf Sicilian. One of the problems with the Najdorf is that "it's useless to play for equality," as GM Yuri Razuvaev put it. That is, Black cannot reach an equal sign by trading a few minor pieces as Capablanca et al did when they defended a Queen's Gambit Declined or a 1 e4 e5 opening. Instead, Black must seek counterplay. That could also be said of many other sharp openings - the King's Indian, Pirc and Benoni Defenses, the Dragon and Sveshnikov Sicilian, and the Winawer Variation of the French Defense to name a few. In this position, which arose from a Najdorf, the only easy way to trade pieces, with 1 ... ♗c4, just hands White ♘f5!. Instead, Black chose the aggressive 1 ... ♘h5!? and ... ♘hf4, which seeks a kingside advantage. (Black doesn't fear ♘xe6 because after ... fxe6 the e6 pawn can't be attacked and it stops ♘d5.) White's reply, 2 ♘d5 ♗xd5 3 exd5, was logical. But after 3 ... ♘hf4 4 ♗f1 ♕d7 5 c4 h5! 6 ♖c1 ♗e5. Black's pieces were better coordinated than his. Black's impending ... g4 offers more than mere counterchances. Black can't stop that push but he can take precautions such as 7 ♕d2 and 7 ... g4 8 h4, to keep the h-file closed. Instead, he went into 7 ♔g1 g4! 8 fxg4 hxg4 9 ♕xg4? ♕xg4 10 hxg4, overlooking 10 ... ♘xd5! (11 cxd5 ♗h2+ 12 ♔h1 ♗f4+). Black had the edge after 11 ♗d3 ♗h2+ 12 ♔f1 ♗f4 13 ♖cd1 ♘f6 and won soon after 14 g3 ♗e5 15 ♗e2 ♘e4! 16 ♘f5 e6! 17 ♗f3 ♘xf2 18 ♖xd6!? ♘h1!. The First Line of Defense Traditional defense set the priorities this way: First, you make sure your king is safe. Second, you need to secure other potential targets. Third - and only then - you can begin looking for ways to make threats and exert pressure. Counterplay is appropriate when you're in trouble. It's the last line of defense. But according to New Defense it's the first line. An illustration comes when the Nimzo-Indian Defense, 1 d4 ♘f6 2 c4 e6 3 ♘c3 ♗b4, is challenged by 4 a3 ♗xc3+ 5 bxc3. White accepts doubled c-pawns in return for freedom to expand in the center. Early efforts at defense tried to exploit the c-pawns - but only after Black was safely castled, e.g., 5 ... c5 6 e3 ♘c6 7 ♗d3 O-O 8 ♘e2 b6 9 e4! ♘e8 10 O-O d6. Black's ... ♘e8 is a prophylactic idea introduced by Capablanca in a similar position. Black retreats so that he can stop a powerful f2-f4-f5! by meeting 11 f4 with 11 ... f5. But in a 1948 game, White found a tactical flaw here: 11 e5! dxe5 12 dxe5 so that 12 ... ♘xe5?? 13 ♗xh7+ and ♕xd8 costs a queen. The strategic point is that the pawn at e5 prevents a Black knight from returning to defend at f6. For example, 12 ... ♗a6 13 ♗f4 ♘a5 goes after the c4-pawn. But White's attack is faster, 14 ♕c2 g6 15 ♗h6 and ♘g3. In the game, Black tried 12 ... ♗b7 13 ♗f4 f5 to contest the kingside. After 14 exf6 he didn't like the looks of 14 ... ♕xf6 15 ♕c2 or 14 ... ♘xf6 (although 15 ♗d6 ♖f7 is fairly harmless). He went in for 14 ... e5 and White won after 15 fxg7! ♖xf4 16 ♘xf4 exf4 17 ♗xh7+! ♔xh7 18 ♕h5+ ♔xg7 19 ♖ad1 ♕f6 20 ♖d7+. Or 19 ... ♕c7 20 ♕g4+ and ♖d7(+). Improvements were found, of course. Black can delay ... d6 in favor of 10 ... ♗a6 and win the c4-pawn after ... ♘d6. And that indicated what Black's real problem was: He was seeking counterplay too late. Today one of the best ways to handle this variation begins with 5 ... b6, rather than 5 ... c5, with quicker pressure against the c4-pawn. A main line runs 5 ... b6 6 f3 ♗a6 7 e4 ♘c6! 8 ♗d3 ♘a5. By not castling Black saves a tempo. But he also denies his opponent a more stationary target, a king at g8. True, he allows 9 e5. But experience has shown that 9 ... ♘g8 offers Black excellent chances. The c4-pawn cannot be defended for long. After 9 ♕e2 Black can play 9 ... d6 followed by ... ♕d7-a4 and, if necessary, ... c5 and ... ♖c8. It is virtually certain Black will achieve his goal - winning the pawn - and put the onus on White. The moral is simple: If you can generate threats, even in the opening, in a part of the board where your opponent is vulnerable, you may distract him from the area where you are weaker. Then his pressure never becomes an initiative. Or his initiative never becomes overwhelming. This is why today's players are willing to delay castling much longer than their predecessors: Granda Zuniga - Lenier Dominguez, Wijk aan Zee 2004
1 d4 d5 2 c4 c6 3 ♘c3 ♘f6 4 ♘f3 e6 5 e3 ♘bd7 6 ♕c2 ♗d6 7 g4 dxc4 8 g5 ♘d5 9
♗xc4 b6 10 ♗d2 ♗b7 11 O-O-O A player of an older generation might have reacted to White's quick g-pawn push with the safety-minded 11 ... ♕c7 and ... O-O-O. But that is unnecessary because Black's king isn't a target yet. However, the White queen and king on the c-file were, after 11 ... ♖c8! and ... c5. White got his king off the file, 12 ♔b1. But 12 ... b5 13 ♗e2 ♘xc3+ was still good, as 14 ♗xc3? b4 15 ♗d2 c5! with an edge would have shown. White played the more accurate 14 ♕xc3 b4 15 ♕d3. But Black would have excellent compensation after 15 ... c5 16 dxc5 ♘xc5 and then 17 ♕b5+ ♗c6 18 ♕xb4 ♘e4 19 ♕d4 O-O. For example, 20 ♗e1 ♕c7 and ... ♗d5, threatening ... ♕c2+, or ... ♖fd8. (Note that the check at move 17 was the only drawback to Black's delay in castling.) Instead of 16 dxc5, White went for 16 b3. Only now did Black play 16 ... O-O. His queenside initiative was well ahead of whatever White had hoped to do on the other wing. Black was headed to victory after 17 ♖hg1 ♕b6 18 ♗c1 ♗d5! 19 e4 c4!. The different attitude of New Defense is reflected in openings with quiet reputations, such as the Caro-Kann Defense. Masters used to assume that Black would have to deal cautiously with pressure until he can equalize. Today Black thinks differently:
1 e4 c6 2 d4 d5 3 ♘c3 dxe4 4 ♘xe4 ♘d7 5 ♗c4 ♘gf6 6 ♘g5 e6 7 ♕e2 ♘b6 8 ♗d3
h6 9 ♘5f3 c5 10 dxc5 ♗xc5 11 ♗d2 O-O 12 O-O-O White has two aggressive ideas: He can centralize one knight, ♘e5, reinforce it with ♘gf3, and then push his protected g-pawn to g5. Or he can go for an open kingside with a quicker g2-g4!. For example, 12 ... ♘bd7 discourages 13 ♘e5 but invites 13 ♘h3 and 14 g4, e.g. 13 ... a6 14 g4! ♘xg4 15 ♖hg1 with a strong attack. After Black lost several times in this variation, annotators blamed the passive Caro-Kann. But the timid 12 ... ♘bd7 was the culprit. The right idea is 12 ... ♘a4!. White's king can be as vulnerable as Black's, e.g. 13 ♘e5? ♕b6! 14 b3? ♗a3+ 15 ♔b1 ♘d5!, threatening a 'family check' on c3. White does better with 13 ♗b5. But retreating the attacked knight is out of the question. Black's best is 13 ... ♗d7, e.g. 14 ♗xd7 ♕xd7! and now the discovered attack on the queen, 15 ♗xh6, allows 15 ... ♕c7 and 16 ... ♕b6. The same goes for 14 ♗xa4 ♗xa4 15 ♗xh6, when Black has compensation after 15 ... ♕b6. Other-Wing Counterplay The defender typically launches action on 'his' wing, the queenside for Black in these last examples. But New Defense also explores counter-intuitive counterplay. White's kingside attack is well ahead of Black's queenside effort and he would have the edge after hxg6. Then ... fxg6 would allow ♗xe5 followed by f6-f7+ while ... hxg6 and ... ♘xg6 invite a heavy piece invasion of the h-file. Black's 1 ... gxh5! was stunning but logical. The only real targets he had to attack were White pawns, at h5, g5 and e4, and this exploits them (2 ♕xh5? ♗xe4). Still, 1 ... gxh5! looked risky. Can Black last long after 2 ♗g2 protects the e-pawn and prepares 3 ♕xh5 and 4 ♖h1 ? The answer came from 2 ... ♘g4!, which threatened ... ♖xg5. Play continued 3 ♕d2 ♕a5 and White set a trap with 4 ♖h1. If 4 ... ♖xg5?, White can cut communication of the heavy pieces with 5 e5! and threaten both 6 ♕xg5+ and 6 ♗xb7. But Black had another surprise, 4 ... h6! so that 5 gxh6 ♗xh6 or 5 ♖xh5 ♖xg5 favors him. White gambled on 5 e5 dxe5 6 ♗b6 instead. But Black's two extra minor pieces were better than White's extra rook after 6 ... ♖xb6 7 ♘xb6 ♕xb6 8 ♖xh5 ♗xg2 9 ♕xg2 ♘e3! 10 ♕g1 ♘d5!. Counterplay = Threats For counterplay to go beyond mere 'clutching' it has to generate threats. This may require ingenuity. White is a pawn ahead and his knights have pawn-protected outposts at e4 and c5 that make them at least as good as Black's bishops. It is easy to visualize White improving his position with ♘bc5 followed by ♕a6 or ♖d1-d6. Or he can play ♖a1 followed by ♘bc5 and ♖a6 or ♖a7. His winning chances would be excellent. Black kick-started his counterplay with a major decision, 1 ... ♗xe4!. His first point was tactical, 2 ♕xe4? ♕a2+ loses a knight. His second point was strategic: after 2 fxe4 there were potential targets at e4 and c3. Clutching followed: 2 ... ♕a3 3 ♕c2 ♕a6 4 ♘c5 ♕c4 5 ♕d3. With 5 ... ♕g8! Black began to go beyond harassment. He prepared ... ♖d8 and could refute 6 ♖d1? with 6 ... ♕a2+! (7 ♔g1 ♕h2+). At first it appears 6 ♕d7 ♖d8 7 ♕xc6 is winning. But 7 ... ♕c4 would get messy. Safer is 6 ♖f2 (to guard against ... ♖d8-d2+). White would remain in control after 6 ... ♖d8 7 ♕a6 ♗e3 8 ♖e2. However, he opted for 6 ♕d7 ♖d8 7 ♕e6? because it seems to shut out the Black queen. But this failed after 7 ... ♖d2+ 8 ♖f2 ♕d8!. It was a good time to head towards a draw with 9 ♖xd2 ♕xd2+ 10 ♔f1 ♕d1+ and a perpetual check. But White didn't appreciate how much counterplay had developed and he allowed 9 ♘b3? ♖d3!. In the face of a killing 10 ... ♖g3+ White tried 10 ♖f3 ♖xf3 11 ♔xf3. But Black picked off three pawns - all with check (11 ... ♕d3+ 12 ♔f2 ♕e3+! 13 ♔f1 ♕f3+ 14 ♔e1 ♕xc3+ etc.) - and won. Counterplay vs. Material Defending a difficult position often seems like taking a school test with a long list of questions about material: Is it worth grabbing a pawn when you know it hands your opponent an initiative? Is it better to make prophylactic moves before seeking a material edge? We'll get to those questions in later chapters. But here's another: Is it better to have counterplay or an extra pawn? Anatoly Karpov gave his answer three times in this game: Kamsky - Karpov, Elista 1996
1 e4 c6 2 d4 d5 3 ♘d2 dxe4 4 ♘xe4 ♘d7 5 ♘g5 ♘gf6 6 ♗d3 e6 7 ♘1f3 ♗d6 8 ♕e2
h6 9 ♘e4 ♘xe4 10 ♕xe4 ♘f6 11 ♕e2 ♕c7 12 ♗d2 b6 13 O-O-O ♗b7 14 ♖he1 O-O 15 g4!? First, he rejected 15 ... ♘xg4? because 16 ♖g1 is too dangerous (16 ... ♘f6 17 ♗xh6). Instead, he used one of the basic weapons we examined in Chapter Four, a strike in the center, 15 ... c5!. Then came 16 g5 hxg5. By getting his b7-bishop into the game Black could punish 17 ♗xg5? with 17 ... ♗f4+! 18 ♔b1? ♗xf3! or 18 ♗xf4 ♕xf4+ (19 ♔b1 ♗xf3 or 19 ♘d2 cxd4). White's 17 ♘xg5 posed a second question for Black. Should he grab another pawn, 17 ... ♗xh2 ? No, he decided. Opening the other half of the h-file was foolhardy when he could limit the coordination of White pieces with 17 ... ♗f4! instead. White posed a third question with 18 h4 and Black went into "a long think," as he put it. Black can take the pawn at d4, perhaps reinforcing it later with a trade of bishops and ... e5. "But the d-file would be closed and the White king would be secure," Karpov said. The White bishop at d3 would be immune to ... c4 and "I would have little counterplay and be on the defensive for quite a while," he added. He preferred 18 ... ♖ad8! to get his rook to the fine d4 square. Then his pieces would be well-coordinated and ... c4 would be in the air. Play went 19 dxc5 bxc5. Black's queen rook would make itself felt after 20 ♖g1 c4! 21 ♗xc4 ♖xd2! 22 ♖xd2 ♗xd2+ (23 ♕xd2 ♕xc4 or 23 ♔xd2 ♕f4+). White avoided that with 20 ♗e3 but then came 20 ... ♖d4!. Black is in better shape for material grabbing than before, thanks to his counterplay, e.g. 21 h5 ♗xe3+ 22 fxe3 ♖h4 followed by ... ♖xh5 or ... c4. White got better mileage out of 21 ♖g1! and then 21 ... ♗xe3+ 22 fxe3 (not 22 ♕xe3 ♕f4!) ♖xh4 23 ♖df1 because of his prospects for ♖xf6. Black organized his pieces well, 23 ... ♕e5 24 ♕f2 ♖h6!. But White was still able to obtain enough play, 25 ♖g3 ♗e4 26 ♖fg1! g6 (26 ... ♗xd3? 27 ♘f3! and ♖xg7+) 27 ♖f1 ♔g7 28 ♕xf6+! ♕xf6 29 ♖xf6 ♗xd3 30 ♖xf7+ to reach a salvageable ending. Sacrifice for Counterplay There's a related puzzle for defenders to solve: If counterplay can be better than pawn-grabbing, what about giving up a pawn to seize the initiative from an attacker? This question taxed 19th century defenders. White had steadily increased the pressure on the c6-pawn during the middlegame and would have continued to do so after 1 ... ♕e8 2 ♖a1 followed by ♖a6-b6. But Black looked for a target and naturally saw b4. He played 1 ... ♖cb7! and then 2 ♖b1 ♖d7!. It was a tacit offer of a pawn - or of a draw by repetition (3 ♖bd1 ♖db7!). White accepted the pawn, 3 ♖xc6, and Black seized the d-file with 3 ... ♖bd8. In the tournament book, Siegbert Tarrasch seemed shocked in the change. Black's position had not been this good "in the entire game," he wrote. When White prepared to push the c-pawn with 4 ♖b6 Black got to make threats, 4 ... f5 5 ♗f3 ♖d4 6 ♕a6 ♕h4! (rather than 6 ... ♖xf4? 7 ♖b7 ♖d7 8 c6!). Black's heavy pieces are active: Now 7 ♖b7+ ♔g8 8 c6? would have been met by 8 ... ♕xf4 9 c7 ♕e3+ 10 ♔f1 ♖d2 11 ♗e2 ♖c8 and Black holds (or 10 ♔h1? ♖d1+!). White did better with 7 g3 ♕f6 8 ♕e2. But 8 ... ♖d3! prepared a strong queen check on d4. Play went 9 c6 ♕d4+ 10 ♔h1 ♖e3 11 ♕f1 ♕c3. Now 12 ♖c1 would fail to 12 ... ♖xf3 13 ♖xc3 ♖xf1+ 14 ♔g2 ♖fd1!. A key point is that 15 c7 ♖8d2+ 16 ♔h3? ♖h1! walks into a mating net. So the Black rooks took over the seventh rank, 12 ♗g2 ♖d2 13 c7 ♖c2! 14 ♖d1 ♖3e2 15 ♕f3 ♖xg2! 16 ♕xc3 ♖xh2+ and drew by perpetual check. Tarrasch added that the turn of events after 3 ... ♖bd8 "is quite a common occurrence." It happens when a player converts positional pressure into a material edge, he said. "A crisis arises in which the strategically lost player obtains the opportunity for counterattack." Tarrasch hinted that a "strategically lost player" didn't deserve this reversal of fortune. But it makes sense: In the first diagram Black's pieces were passively tied to the defense of the c6-pawn. Once freed from that duty, they came into their own. Here's how this works in a modern opening, the Dragon Sicilian. Black had fared badly when this position arose in previous games because he tried to defend the backward pawn at d6. After 1 ... ♖c6 2 ♘a5 ♖b6 3 ♘c4 he is only helping White. But in this game he just ignored the threat, 1 ... ♖ec8! 2 ♕xd6 ♗e6. Black's rooks and bishop were then ready to make threats of their own. And after 3 c3 b5: White no longer has a target at d6. Instead, he faces the prospect of ... a5-a4 or ... b4. Play went 4 ♕d2 a5 and now 5 ♘xa5 ♖a4 6 ♘b3 ♖8a8 7 ♘c1 ♗xa2+ would be fine for Black. But he also had compensation in the game after 5 ♕g5 ♕xg5 6 hxg5 a4. He sailed into a draw after 7 ♘d2 ♖4c7 8 a3 ♖d7 9 ♔c1 f6 10 gxf6+ ♔xf6 11 ♘f1 ♖xd1+ 12 ♔xf1 ♖d8+ and ... ♔g5. The initiative-stealing sacrifice is another version of changing the nature of the struggle. An opponent who wins a weak pawn may feel that he is winning the game. That often lures him into carelessness: Both players have pawns to attack, at d7 and e5. But Black is behind in development and that would cost him after 1 ... ♘xe5? 2 ♘xe5 ♗xe5 3 ♗xe5 ♕xe5? 4 ♘d6+ and White wins. If he tries to protect d7 with 1 ... ♕a7 and ... ♖d8 - so that he can castle without hanging the d-pawn - White would get an edge from 2 c4! bxc4 3 ♕xc4 ♖d8 and then 4 h4! ♘c6 5 ♘c5. Black would love to play the blocking 1 ... ♘d5 but 2 ♖xd5! is dangerous (2 ... exd5 3 ♘d6+ ♗xd6 4 exd6+ ♔f8 5 ♘h4! ♘xh4 6 ♗xh4 with strong compensation and a much easier position for White to play than Black). Black solved his problem with 1 ... O-O!, abandoning the d-pawn. After White grabbed it, 2 ♖xd7, he brought his knight to an outpost, 2 ... ♘d5. White has no obvious goal now that the d-pawn is gone. Moreover, his e-pawn is threatened and his rook, now behind enemy lines, may be embarrassed by ... ♕c8. White chose the natural 3 ♘d6. It rules out ... ♕c8 and ... ♘xe5. It also prepares an attack on f7 using the rook and both knights. White had a positional edge previously so the attack seems to make sense. But after 3 ... ♘b6! White had to sacrifice, 4 ♖xf7 ♖xf7 5 ♘xf7 ♔xf7. Even though chances were roughly equal after 6 ♘g5+ ♔g8! (6 ... ♔e7 7 ♕g4) 7 ♘xe6 ♕c8 and 8 ♕g4 ♖a7!, White was soon outplayed and lost, 9 ♖d1 ♘a4! 10 h3 ♘xb2 11 ♖d5 ♗b6 12 ♖d6 ♘c4. Three becomes Two We usually think of counterplay as a feature of middlegames. But changing a trend at the cost of material can also alter the likely outcomes of an early endgame. Once again this has a psychological benefit. Black threatens 1 ... ♖xa2+ and there is no good way to defend the pawn (1 ♔b2 ♖b8+ 2 ♔a1?? ♘b3+). Of course, losing the a-pawn would simply make material equal. But with the only passed pawn and more active pieces than White's, Black would have reasonable winning chances. "All three outcomes are possible," grandmasters say - meaning a win, a loss or a draw. White changed that with 1 ♖xa5! ♖xa5 2 ♗xc4. Then he had two pawns and a strong bishop for a rook. With his king to shepherd the a-pawn and a potentially passed d-pawn, his winning chances had risen and his losing chances had dropped. Two outcomes - a White win or a draw - were much more likely. Black appreciated that and played 2 ... h5 3 h4 ♖c8! 4 ♔d3. Now on 4 ... ♖a3+ 5 ♗b3 White would have excellent winning chances once he creates a passed d-pawn (6 e4 and 7 d5). Black defended with 4 ... ♖xc4! 5 ♔xc4 ♖xa2. White now had an extra pawn in a rook endgame. But there was no passed pawn and Black's rook was very active. The most likely outcome, by far, was a draw. After 25 more moves that was the result. We saw a related phenomenon when we considered Lasker's aggressive approach in Chapter Three. He sought to turn a position with two likely results - a loss and a draw - into one with three outcomes. Typically, this requires the defender to choose the best practical move over the objectively best move. A pawn down and facing two bishops, Black's best drawing chance lies in trading pawns. After 1 ... cxd4! 2 ♕xd4 ♘c5 White is a long way from making progress. Better is 2 exd4 but the d-pawn will be held in check by ... ♘f6 followed by ... ♖d8. But Black chose the objectively inferior 1 ... c4!? because it creates a passed pawn and potential winning chances. It also poses some difficult choices for White. After the game he concluded that he should have played 2 ♕e4 ♘f6 3 ♕c2 followed by pushing his e-pawn. But psychologically it is very hard, in such an apparently favorable position, to retreat like that instead of taking a second pawn with 2 ♗xh7+? ♔xh7 3 ♕e4+ ♔g8 4 ♕xe7. But Black's passed pawn grew in power after 4 ... c3!. For example, 5 a4 c2 6 ♗a3 would be met by 6 ... ♖b8 followed by ... ♖b1 or ... ♕c6 and ... ♕xa4. White tried instead to push his center pawns, 5 e4 c2! 6 d5 but they were less threatening than the c-pawn after 6 ... ♖b8, Play went 7 g3 ♕c8 8 ♕g5 ♕c4 9 f3 ♖b3 with the idea of ... ♕d4+ and ... ♘e5. White couldn't adjust to the idea that he might lose. He played 10 ♕d2? ♖d3 11 ♕e2 ♘e5 12 ♔g2 ♖d1! and lost after 13 ♕xc4 ♘xc4 14 d6 ♔f8 15 a4 ♔e8 16 ♔f2 ♘d2! 17 ♖e1 ♘xf3!. Is active always better than solid? No, some players will feel more comfortable and have better results with moves like 1 ... cxd4. And in some positions, solid will outscore active nine times out of ten. But you should still be looking for counterplay as your first line of defense. Quiz What should Black be thinking? Where does Black's best chance for counterplay lie? White threatens to win a pawn with 1 gxh6. After 1 ... hxg5 2 ♕xg5 the impending 3 h6 retains his winning chances. What can Black do? Chapter Six: Risk Management Risk is bad, according to traditional defense. It should be reduced to the minimum. Of course, this point of view recognized that risk couldn't be eliminated entirely. But a position with a 20 percent chance of losing was considered good - even though there might only be a 20 percent of winning. That situation was preferable, according to the conventional wisdom, to a highly double-edged position with a 40 percent chance of losing - even if there is a 50 percent chance of winning. This, too, has changed. Today's masters are more willing to take an acceptable risk. What is acceptable? Defining that has become as important as identifying risk itself. Zhang Pengxiang - Khalifman, Shanghai 2001
1 e4 c6 2 d4 d5 3 e5 ♗f5 4 ♘c3 e6 5 g4 ♗g6 6 ♘ge2 c5 7 h4 h5 8 ♘f4 ♘c6 9 ♘xg6
fxg6 10 ♘e2 ♕b6 11 ♘f4 cxd4 12 ♗d3 ♘xe5 13 ♘xg6 ♘xg6 14 ♗xg6+ ♔d7 15 gxh5 ♗b4+ 16 c3 dxc3 17 ♕a4+ ♔e7 18 bxc3 ♗xc3+ 19 ♔e2 Black appreciated that 19 ... ♗xa1 20 ♗a3+ was dangerous. But he knew that if he survived, his extra material would win easily. Should he calculate 19 ... ♗xa1 as far as he can see? Or is it better to look for mating chances against White? Should he investigate 19 ... ♘f6, instead? And what about offering a trade of queens with 19 ... ♕d4 ? Pragmatism is a good guide here. Black realized that the simple and solid 19 ... ♕c6 made sense. After 20 ♕xc6 bxc6 his risk of losing would be tiny - and his winning chances would be much greater than if he damaged his pawn with 19 ... ♕a6+ 20 ♕xa6 bxa6. He also had to evaluate his chances if White avoided the endgame, (19 ... ♕c6) 20 ♕a3+ ♔d7 or 20 ♗a3+ ♔d7 21 ♕f4 ♘f6. Of course, he might lose due to a blunder in a complex position then. But his winning chances would be much greater than his losing chances. In the end Black chose 19 ... ♕c6 and won the endgame after 20 ♕xc6 bxc6 21 ♖b1 ♔d6 22 ♗a3+ c5. He had found an acceptable - and successful - degree of risk. Comfort Zone Every defender wants to find his or her comfort zone. But what may seem safe to one player may not feel safe to another. There are many positions that are objectively safe because that's what a superior player - or a computer - says. But they may not be acceptable to you. With h4-h5 and ♘f6+ coming up Black took the common-sense precaution 1 ... ♔f8. We'll consider moves like that - what we call prophylactic - in more detail in Chapter Eight. For the time being, let's consider Black's comfort zone. He later found that a computer recommended 1 ... ♘xe5 and 2 ♘f6+ ♔h8. For example, 3 ♖xe5 can be met by 3 ... ♗xf3. And 3 ♘xe5 could lead to 3 ... gxf6 4 ♗xf5 exf5 and 5 ... fxe5. "But of course I wouldn't dream of this," Black added. Few humans would. The risk is outside their comfort zone. There are many positions on which humans disagree about what is acceptable risk. Morozevich - Gelfand, Biel 2009
1 e4 e5 2 ♗c4 ♘f6 3 d3 c6 4 ♘f3 d5 5 ♗b3 ♗d6 6 exd5 ♘xd5 7 O-O O-O 8 ♖e1 ♘d7
9 d4 exd4 10 ♗xd5 cxd5 11 ♕xd4 ♗c5
A previous grandmaster game went 12 ♕xd5 ♕b6! and 13 ♖e2 ♘f6 14 ♕b3 ♕a6 15 ♘c3 ♗e6 16 ♕a4. White eventually won - but perhaps not because the gambit was unsound. In this game White considered 12 ♕xd5 only briefly because it seemed to him that Black had plenty of compensation. That doesn't mean that Black would be better after 12 ♕xd5. White just felt that his opponent would have enough play to make his life unpleasant for several moves. It's clear in the diagram that Black is going to gain time with ... ♕b6 no matter what White does. He looked at 12 ♕d3 and realized it would give him a small edge, in view of the isolated d5-pawn, but with very little risk. White was the one making threats after 12 ... ♕b6 13 ♖e2 ♘f6 14 ♘c3 ♗g4 15 ♘a4 ♕c6, as the game went, and eventually won the ending after 16 ♘xc5 ♕xc5 17 ♗e3 ♕c4 18 ♖d2 ♕xd3 19 ♖xd3. Tal's Credo Mikhail Tal helped overthrow the traditional view of risk. "A chess player sacrifices a piece for attack," he wrote in a celebrated article. "Does he risk? Of course, because the attack could be repelled." Then Tal asked a second question: What about his opponent? "A chess player accepts the sacrifice (even though he could decline), hoping to repel the attack. Does he risk? Of course, because the attack can be irresistible." "Whose risk is greater? There's no device that can measure that clearly," Tal concluded. Let's see how two great modern players evaluate risk: Anand - Ivanchuk, Belgrade 1997
1 e4 e5 2 ♘f3 ♘c6 3 ♗b5 a6 4 ♗a4 ♘f6 5 O-O b5 6 ♗b3 ♗b7 7 ♖e1 ♗c5 8 c3 O-O 9
d4 ♗b6 10 ♗e3 cxd4 11 exd4 ♘a5 12 ♗g5 ♘xb3 13 axb3 h6 14 ♗h4 g5 Black's last move met the threat of 15 e5. Was it risky? To properly answer that you have to measure it against a safer-looking alternative. But is 14 ... d6 really safer? It doesn't appear so in view of 15 ♘bd2 followed by ♕c2 and the renewed threat of e4-e5. To judge 15 ♘xg5 correctly we have to examine the alternative, 15 ♗g3 and the natural response 15 ... ♘xe4!. And when we can't find compensation for White's lost pawn then, we should look for compensation instead after the piece sacrifice, 15 ♘xg5 hxg5 16 ♗xg5. The threat of 17 e5 offers plenty of 'comp,' as 16 ... d6 17 ♖e3 ♖e8 18 ♖f3 indicates. This explains why White chose 15 ♘xg5! - and Black replied 15 ... ♘xe4!. A quick look indicates 16 ♖xe4? ♗xe4 is unsound, e.g. 17 ♕h5 hxg5 18 ♗xg5 f6 or 18 ... ♗g6. So he had to content himself with 16 ♘xe4 ♕xh4 17 ♘bc3. Material is still equal. The most striking features of the position are Black's two bishops and ruptured kingside. Can he afford to take a new risk, say with 17 ... b4 ? After a brief examination of 18 g3! Black backed off (18 ... ♕h3 19 ♘f6+ ♔- moves 20 ♘cd5 or 18 ... ♕d8 19 ♕g4+ ♔h8 20 ♕h5 or 19 ... ♔h7 20 ♕f5+ with a strong attack). Instead he opted for the safety of 17 ... ♔h8. His bishops and play along the g- file were enough to grant him equal chances after 18 ♘c5 ♗c6 19 g3 ♖g8 20 ♖e5! ♖g4! 21 ♘5e4 ♖ag8. A draw was reached at move 32. 1 e4 e6 3 d4 d5 3 ♘c3 dxe4 4 ♘xe4 ♘d7 5 ♘f3 ♘gf6 6 ♗d3 c5 7 ♘xf6+ ♘xf6 8

In the diagram, the natural 13 ♘e5 invites 13 ... ♕xb2 14 O-O followed perhaps by ♘c4/♗c3. But it is refuted by 13 ... ♗xf2+! 14 ♕xf2 ♕xb2 and Black will be at least one safe pawn ahead. The most appealing alternative is 13 O-O-O. Some computers regard 13 ... ♗xf2 (or 13 ... ♘g4 first) as safe for Black. But GM Alexander Morozevich gave 13 O-O-O a question mark because it allows a strong sacrifice by Black, 13 ... e5!. For example, 14 ♘xe5 ♖e8 15 ♘c4 ♕a6! is fine for Black, as is 14 ♕xe5 ♘g4. White's best in the diagram may be 13 O-O after which 13 ... e5! (14 ♘xe5 ♖e8 15 ♘c4 ♕d8 16 ♕f3 ♗g4 17 ♕xb7 ♖b8) offers compensation. Change of Mind The New Defender takes more risks than his predecessors. But unless he burns his bridges, he can change his mind about how much risk he accepts: Topalov - Kramnik, Novgorod 1997
1 e4 c5 2 ♘f3 ♘c6 3 d4 cxd4 4 ♘xd4 ♘f6 5 ♘c3 d6 6 ♗c4 ♕b6 7 ♘xc6 bxc6 8 O-O
g6 9 e5 dxe5 10 ♕e2 ♕d4 11 ♗e3 ♕d6 12 ♖ad1 ♕c7 13 f4 ♗g4 14 ♕f2 If Black wanted to defend the e-pawn with ... ♕c7 why didn't he do it at the 10th move? Because he wanted to lure White into the tempo-gaining ♖ad1, which allows ... ♗g4 after 13 f4. But in the diagram Black had second thoughts. He looked at 14 ... ♗xd1 and didn't feel comfortable with what he'd face after 15 fxe5! ♕xe5 16 ♗d4. It wasn't too late. He took the cautious way out with 14 ... e4!, limiting the number of open lines. White had nothing better than to try to regain his lost pawn with 15 ♖de1 and Black replied 15 ... ♗f5! 16 h3 h5. White had to swap down to regain his pawn, 17 ♗d4 ♗g7 18 b3 O-O 19 ♗xf6 ♗xf6 20 ♘xe4. After 20 ... ♗xe4! 21 ♖xe4 ♕b6 he accepted Black's draw offer in view of the coming bishops-of-opposite-color ending. As Tal pointed out, the psychology of sacrifice cuts two ways. Yes, taking material forces you to defend. But it also forces your opponent to prove he has compensation. Who, then, is under greater pressure?
1 e4 c5 2 ♘f3 ♘c6 3 d4 cxd4 4 ♘xd4 ♘f6 5 ♘c3 d6 6 g3 ♘xd4 7 ♕xd4 g6 8 ♗b5+
♗d7 9 ♗xd7+ ♕xd7 10 ♗g5 ♗g7 11 ♘d5 White threatens to win a pawn with captures on f6. When this position first occurred, Black either got insufficient compensation for a lost pawn after 11 ... O-O? 12 ♘xf6+ or a poor game positionally after 11 ... e5. So attention turned to 11 ... ♘h5. Now it's White's turn to consider a gambit. The riskless 12 ♕b4 leads to nothing after 12 ... e6!. But if the queen retreats to d3 or d2 instead of b4, can Black safely take on b2? The answer is yes. After 12 ♕d3 ♗xb2! 13 ♖b1 ♗f6 Black prompts a trade of pieces. True, he will face pressure after 14 ♗xf6 ♘xf6 15 ♕d4 or 15 ♕c3. But the onus will be on White to prove he has a pawn's worth of play. In one GM game, 15 ♕c3, which threatens ♘c7+ as well as ♘xf6+, was answered by 15 ... ♖c8! (not 15 ... O-O?? 16 ♕xf6! exf6 17 ♘xf6+ and ♘xd7). Then 16 ♘xf6+ gxf6 17 ♕xf6 regains the pawn. But White has weak pawns that would cost him the initiative, after 17 ... O-O 18 ♕b2 b6 19 O-O ♕c6, for example. White went for 16 ♕xf6 and 16 ... exf6 17 ♘xf6+ ♔e7 18 ♘xd7. But the position became drawish after 18 ... ♖xc2 19 O-O ♔xd7 20 ♖xb7+ ♖c7. Another game went 15 ♕d4 (instead of 15 ♕c3). Black could afford 15 ... b6 and then 16 O-O ♕d8 17 ♖b3 O-O 18 ♘xf6+ gxf6. Black couldn't keep his pawn but after 19 ♖f3 ♖c8 20 ♖xf6 ♖xc2 21 ♖xd6 ♕c8 chances were roughly equal. Black eventually won. In these cases, grabbing a pawn led to a temporary White initiative that quickly turned sour. But Tal's credo raises another question: Is there a cost of not grabbing? When Refusing is Risky Traditional defense warned against a pawn grab that would open files or diagonals leading to your king. That is sound as far as it goes. But that's not far enough. It doesn't take into account the possibility that those lines are going to be forced open, whether you like it or not.
1 e4 c6 2 d4 d5 3 ♘c3 dxe4 4 ♘xe4 ♗f5 5 ♘g3 ♗g6 6 h4 h6 7 ♘f3 ♘d7 8 h5 ♗h7 9
♗d3 ♗xd3 10 ♕xd3 e6 11 ♗f4 ♘gf6 12 O-O-O ♗e7 13 ♘e4 O-O Here 14 g4 has been tried. White plans 15 ♘xf6+ ♘xf6 16 g5! or 15 ... ♗xf6 16 ♖dg1 and 17 g5!. In either case at least half of the g-file is opened and White enjoys good attacking chances. But 14 g4? fell out of favor when it was found that 14 ... ♘xg4! is safe in view of 15 ♖dg1 f5!. For example, 16 ♕b3 ♖f7! 17 ♘d6 ♗xd6 18 ♗xd6 and now 18 ... ♘b6 19 ♕xe6 ♕d7 reaches a nice endgame (20 ♕xd7 ♖xd7 21 ♗g3 ♖e8). In this case, grabbing was safer than ignoring the g-pawn. But suppose White improves with the immediate 14 ♘xf6+! ♘xf6 and only then 15 g4: Once again Black has to evaluate the risk of grabbing - and the risk of not grabbing. Experience has shown that 15 ... ♕a5 is well met by 16 g5! hxg5 17 ♗xg5. White would have a solid attack after 18 ♖dg1 or 18 h6 - even if Black decides to take a pawn on the other wing, 17 ... ♕xa2. Here again the lesser risk comes from 15 ... ♘xg4!. Then on 16 ♖hg1! Black should avoid 16 ... ♘xf2 17 ♕d2! ♘xd1 18 ♗xh6 (18 ... ♗f6 19 ♗xg7 ♗xg7 20 ♕h6). Safety comes from 16 ... f5!. For example, 17 ♕e2 ♔h7 18 ♕xe6 ♕d5 19 ♕xe7 ♕xf3 with equal chances. A defender's best policy may turn out to be a slight sacrifice of his own: Black's last move was ... ♗h3. A bishop trade would: (a) Eliminate White's pressure against his best target, the pawn at b7, (b) Limit White's control of key light squares such as d5 and e4 and allow Black to open the center with ... ♘f6 and ... d5, and (c) Enable Black to mount a kingside attack, such as with ... ♘f6 followed by ... ♕g4-h5, ... ♘g4 and ... ♖af8. White's kingside is vulnerable because its best defensive piece was the now-missing bishop. White appreciated these factors when he played 1 ♗xb7 and 1 ... ♗xf1 2 ♖xf1. This is technically a sacrifice, the Exchange for a pawn. But for a master, it's a no- risk no-brainer. Of course, 1 ♗xb7! is good. A rule of thumb holds that you can't mate a fianchetto king position if you lack a bishop of the same color as the fianchettoed one. After 2 ... ♘f6 3 ♘c3 Black didn't even try with 3 ... ♕h3. Following 4 ♗g2 and h2-h3/♘e4 White would have more than enough protection. Tal's question returns once more. Who has taken a risk? Black's losing chances have soared because of the impending collapse of his queenside after ♗g2/♘c6. On the other hand, his winning chances have risen only slightly. He tried for attack with 3 ... ♔h8 4 ♘e4 ♘g4. But White comfortably created a light-square fortress with 5 ♗d5 ♖f5 6 h3 ♘h6 7 ♔g2 ♖df8 8 f3!. Black's rooks would just become vulnerable after, say, 8 ... ♖h5 9 g4 ♖h4 10 ♕g5! ♕d8 11 ♕xd8 ♖xd8 12 ♔g3!. After 9 g4 he tried 9 ... ♖xh3?! 10 ♔xh3 ♖xf3+ 11 ♖xf3 ♕xg4+ 12 ♔h2 ♕xf3 and should have lost after 13 ♗e6! ♘f5 14 ♗xf5 gxf5 15 ♘g3. Against a good attacker, it will take more than one good decision to navigate the middlegame safely. You may have to evaluate risk at several points. That's what Black faced in this example: Shirov - Timman, Carlsbad 2008

9 O-O ♗e7 10 ♖d1 ♕c8 11 ♗g5 h6
White's gambit came into fashion when it was appreciated that Black can equalize easily after 7 O-O cxd4 8 ♘xd4 ♗xg2. In the diagram White would get some compensation from 12 ♗xe7 ♘xe7 13 e4 followed by ♘c3, ♖d2 and doubling rooks on the d-file. But he opted for the more ambitious 12 ♖xd5. The first point is that 12 ... ♗xd5 13 ♗xe7 ♔xe7 14 ♘c3 would leave Black behind in development and with an insecure king for several moves. Black opted for 12 ... hxg5! instead. He would have a much easier game to play after 13 ♖d2 d5 and still be ahead material. White renewed the offer with 13 ♘c3!. Black realized that defending would be difficult if he had to give up his light-squared bishop for the d5-knight. Play went 13 ... ♘c6 14 ♖ad1 ♘b4 15 ♕e4. Here 15 ... ♘xd5 16 ♘xd5 would virtually force 16 ... ♗xd5 17 ♕xd5 and give White a promising position (17 ... ♕c6 18 ♘e5 ♕xd5 19 ♗xd5). However, White can't withdraw the Exchange offer. Black minimized his risk before taking on d5, with 15 ... ♔f8! and prepared ... ♗f6xc3. Since ... hxg5 was played he didn't need to castle to develop his h8-rook. White tried 16 ♘e5 and Black replied 16 ... ♘xd5 17 ♘xd5 ♗f6. White would still have had compensation after, say, 18 ♕f5, but his pressure was slowly eliminated after 18 ♘c4 ♕c6 19 ♕g4 ♖d8! 20 ♘de3 d5! (21 ♖xd5 ♕c8; 21 ♘xd5 ♕d7) and he lost. Risk-Taking After You've Equalized When you're playing the Black pieces and have gotten through the middlegame unscathed, the position may seem destined to end in a draw. If you want more than a half point, you're going to face a new question: How much risk can you accept to play for win? White's initiative was over and he played 1 ♗xc6. Black could take stock: The natural 1 ... ♕xc6 would give White a very slight edge. Barring a blunder, the most likely result is a draw. If you have to put it in numbers, it might be: an 80 percent of a draw, 12 percent of a White win, 8 percent of a Black win. But the only alternative is 1 ... bxc6!?. Then the c-pawn is a potential target. But so is the b2-pawn. And since White is almost certain to protect his pawn with 2 b3, Black could play 2 ... ♖ed8 and try to make something of his control of the board's only open file. For example, 3 ♖ad1 ♖d5! 4 ♖xd5 cxd5 is a position that favors Black because White's queenside pawns are targets. Maybe it is still an '80 percent draw.' But Black would have the greater of the winning chances. Instead, play went 3 ♖ac1 ♖d5 4 ♕e3 a5 5 ♕c3 ♖ad8. A draw would be even more likely after 6 ♕xc6 ♕xc6 7 ♖xc6 ♖xe5 8 ♔f1!. But White, perhaps convinced that the isolated Black pawns favored him, played 6 h3?. He was mis-evaluating his own risk. There followed 6 ... c5 7 ♖e4 h6 8 ♖c4. White had underestimated the power of the Black rooks after 8 ... ♖d3! 9 ♕e1 ♖8d5 with a threat of ... ♕d7 and ... ♖d1. White should have tried to swap rooks with ♖4c3 at some point but gradually slipped into a position that grew worse and worse: 10 ♔h2 ♕b6 11 ♖1c2 ♕c7 12 ♖a4? ♖d1!. Suddenly White's king was a factor (13 ♕xa5 ♕xe5+ 14 f4 ♕e3). The final act began with 13 ♕c3 ♖xe5! 14 ♕xa5? (rather than 14 ♕g3). The quiet 14 ... ♕d6! set up a devastating discovered check (or 15 ♕a8+ ♔h7 16 ♖a6 ♖h1+! 17 ♔xh1 ♖e1 mate). White resigned after 15 g3 ♔h7 16 ♕a8 ♕d3 17 ♖ac4 ♖d5! in view of a mate after ... ♕f3 or ... ♕f1. We'll consider risk in more detail when we examine sacrifices in the next chapter. But for now let's see what we've learned. Quiz White's attack will be close to decisive if he can play f4-f5. How does Black minimize his risk of losing? Black threatens to blow open the kingside with 1 ... gxh3 or 1 ... h4. The basic weapon of line closing, 1 h4, invites a sacrifice on h4. Should White allow it? Chapter Seven: Sacrifice The only way to refute a sacrifice is to accept it, traditional defense declared. But this rule put defenders at an enormous disadvantage: They took every piece that was thrown at them 'on principle.' One of the best defenders of his era, Amos Burn, famously paid the price in this game: Marshall - Burn, Ostend 1907
1 d4 ♘f6 2 ♘f3 d6 3 ♗f4 ♘bd7 4 e3 g6 5 ♗d3 ♗g7 6 ♘bd2 O-O 7 h4? ♖e8 8 h5
♘xh5! 9 ♖xh5? gxh5 The natural follow-up to White's speculation was 10 ♗xh7+!. Black grabbed the bishop 'on principle' and was mated after 10 ... ♔xh7?? 11 ♘g5+ (11 ... ♔g8 12 ♕xh5 or 11 ... ♔g6 12 ♘df3 e5 13 ♘h4+ ♔f6 14 ♘h7+!). But Black would have had enough material to win after 10 ... ♔f8! (11 ♘g5 ♘f6 12 ♘de4 ♗g4 or 11 ♘h4 e5). Good modern defenders never accept material automatically. They recognize that some sacrifices cannot be refuted and that acceptance can be fatal. When weighing whether to accept, they rely primarily on calculation, common sense and their sense of acceptable risk. Black's last move, ... g6, was designed to keep a White knight off f5 and h5. He can meet the 1 ♗h6 mating threat with 1 ... ♗f6. But White played 1 ♘h5 anyway. Black had to choose between accepting the knight or making an ugly but far-from-fatal concession, 1 ... f6. He was willing to invest time in calculating 1 ... gxh5. It wasn't hard to see that White would reply 2 e5, intending 3 ♕e4 or 3 ♕d3 and a quick mate after ♕xh7+. Clearly 2 ... f5, to block the d3-h7 diagonal, would fail to 3 exf6 ♗xf6 4 ♖xe8+ and ♕xf6. More critical would be 2 ... ♗f8 and then 3 ♕d3 ♗g7. Black might not be able to calculate 4 ♕xh7+ ♔f8 5 ♗h6 to a conclusion. But that would put him out of his comfort zone. He took a deeper look at 2 e5. And he spotted a resource. He can create an escape route for his king and also force matters with 2 ... ♗f6!, a move that White may have overlooked when he considered 1 ♘h5. White had to go ahead with 3 ♕e4 ♖xe5! 4 ♕xh7+. But after 4 ... ♔f8 5 ♗h6+ ♔e7 the e-file was effectively blocked. White tried to force it open with 6 f4 and then 6 ... ♖e6 7 ♗f5 (no better is 7 ♗g6 ♕e8). But 7 ... ♕h8! did the trick. After 8 ♗xe6 ♗xe6! (not 8 ... ♕xh7 9 ♗f5+ and ♗xh7) Black would have two good minor pieces for a rook and would win the 9 ♕xh8 ♖xh8 10 ♗g5 ♗xg5 endgame. White kept his attack alive with 9 ♖xe6+! ♔xe6 10 ♖e1+. But blocking the file, 10 ... ♗e5!, works again because 11 ♕xh8 ♖xh8 12 ♗g5+ f6 is another lost ending. White tried 11 ♕c2. Black consolidated with 11 ... ♕xh6 12 fxe5 ♖c8! 13 exd6+ ♔xd6 14 ♕d3+ ♘d5 and won when the checks ran out. The Vulnerable Sacrificer When a player sacrifices he tries to put his opponent under emotional pressure. But what about the sacrificer? What pressure is he subjected to? To begin with, he has to justify his surrender of material. That may lead to misfires when the sacrifice begins to look dubious. Spassky - Suetin, Soviet Spartakiad 1964
1 d4 d5 2 c4 dxc4 3 ♘f3 ♘f6 4 e3 e6 5 ♗xc4 c5 6 O-O a6 7 ♕e2 b5 8 ♗b3 ♗b7 9
♖d1 ♘bd7 10 ♘c3 ♕c7 11 e4 cxd4 12 e5 dxc3 13 exf6 ♘xf6 14 ♘e5 ♗c5 15 ♗f4 ♕b6 The forcing sequence begun by 12 e5 gave White a lead in development. But he's under pressure to use it. After 16 bxc3 O-O Black would be a safe pawn up. White felt 16 ♘xf7 was the only way to keep his initiative alive. Black quickly saw that 16 ... ♔xf7 would lose to 17 ♖d6!. For example, 17 ... ♗xd6 18 ♕xe6+ ♔f8 18 ♕f7 mate and 18 ... ♔g6 18 ♗c2+ ♔h5 19 ♕h3 mate. Black also understood the power of 16 ... O-O!. Yes, castling is legal. Black is still a pawn ahead but he has dramatically changed the nature of the struggle. His king is safe and he has 17 ... ♘d5, 17 ... ♘e4 and 17 ... cxb2 at his disposal, not to mention 17 ... ♖xf7. White rejected 17 ♘d6 ♗d5 and 17 ♗xe6 ♖xf7 and tried to prepare a more effective capture on e6 with 17 ♘g5?. This was easily handled by 17 ... cxb2! 18 ♖ab1 ♖ae8!. Then captures on e6 have no punch (19 ♘xe6 ♖f7 and 19 ♗xe6+ ♔h8). White limped onward with 19 ♖xb2 ♕c6 20 ♘f3 but Black was already winning after 20 ... ♘e4!. In fact, back at the previous diagram there was a similar scenario: If White had tried 16 bxc3 O-O and then 17 ♘xf7 Black would have gotten a fine game from 17 ... ♘e4 or 17 ... ♘d5. Many sacrifices, like 16 ♘xf7, have hidden weaknesses. They are mistakenly based on the defender accepting the offer - or accepting a second and third follow- up offer. But the defender can say yes, yes - and then no. After White had taken aim at h6 with ♗d2 and ♕c1, Black replied ... ♕a7. Should White retreat the attacked rook from b6? It doesn't look promising for him after 1 ♖b1 ♘xd4. White opted for 1 ♗xh6 and Black called his bluff with 1 ... ♕xb6! 2 ♗xg7 ♔xg7. At first, it looks like White gambled on 3 ♕g5, which is easily handled by 3 ... ♖h8 and 4 ♕f6+ ♔g8. But White had seen further: his second sacrifice, 3 ♘h5+!, would win after 3 ... ♔g8 4 ♕h6. Black had to choose between 3 ... ♔h7 4 ♘g5+ ♔g8 and the acceptance, 3 ... gxh5. The former is hard to calculate after 5 ♘xe6 and 6 ♕h6. The latter is based on 3 ... gxh5 4 ♕g5+ ♔h8? 5 ♕h6+ and mates. Black chose the latter and play went 3 ... gxh5 4 ♕g5+ ♘g6! 5 ♕f6+ ♔g8. White played 6 ♗xg6, hoping for 6 ... fxg6 7 ♕xg6+ But Black shot back 6 ... ♕d8!. Down a rook, White's only resource was more checks: 7 ♗h7+ ♔xh7 8 ♘g5+ ♔g8 9 ♕h6, which gave him two mating threats, ♕h7 and ♘h7-f6+. But the arrival of a reinforcement, 9 ... ♗d3!, was sufficient. White resigned soon after 10 ♖e3 ♕b6! (threatening ... ♕b1+) 11 h3 h4! 12 ♕xh4 ♗g6. The Joys of Pawn Grabbing The philosophy of sacrifice in the 19th century taught that gambits were generally sound because a few extra tempi were worth a pawn. Players like Paul Morphy were even willing to gambit all of their kingside pawns, with 1 e4 e5 2 f4 exf4 3 ♘f3 ♗e7 4 ♗c4 ♗h4+ and 5 g3?! fxg3 6 O-O gxh2+ 7 ♔h1. In the era of New Defense we learned that some gambits - such as the Poisoned Pawn Variation of the Sicilian Defense, 1 e4 c5 2 ♘f3 d6 3 d4 cxd4 4 ♘xd4 ♘f6 5 ♘c3 a6 6 ♗g5 e6 7 f4 ♕b6 and then 8 ♕d2 ♕xb2 - can be safely accepted. Grandmasters predicted that in the 21st century computer analysis would make gambits a thing of the past. They didn't go away. In fact, new gambits arose, including some in queenside openings such as 1 d4 ♘f6 2 c4 e6 3 ♘f3 b6 4 g3 ♗a6 5 ♕c2 ♗b7 6 ♗g2 c5 7 d5!? exd5 8 cxd5 ♘xd5. So where does that leave us? The best conclusion was known in the 19th century: a pawn is usually worth a little trouble. This is particularly true when the defender isn't forced to meet several specific threats. Brinckmann - Nimzovich, Berlin 1927
1 e4 ♘c6 2 d4 d5 3 exd5 ♕xd5 4 ♗e3 e6 5 ♗e2!? ♕xg2! 6 ♗f3 ♕g6 7 ♘e2
If we measure only time and space, White has excellent compensation for a pawn. He will gain more time by harassing the Black queen with moves such as ♖g1. Once he is castled queenside White may be able to profitably blast open the center with d4-d5. But White had no immediate threats. So Black could take time for 7 ... ♘b4! 8 ♘a3 ♘d5. The loss of two tempi was not as important as fixing the problem of the poorly placed knight and forestalling d4-d5. White continued 9 ♘c4 and Black surrendered two more tempi, 9 ... ♕f6! 10 ♘g3 ♕d8, to fix another problem, his misplaced, easily harassed queen. If chess was purely arithmetic, the numerical superiority of White's developed pieces over Black's would be decisive. But there are no exploitable Black weaknesses. White tried 11 ♕d2 ♘gf6 12 ♘e5 c6 13 O-O and Black won without incident following 13 ... ♗d6. Could White improve? Clearly 13 c4?? ♗b4 is out of the question and 13 O-O-O ♗b4 14 c3 ♗d6 and ... ♕a5 is insufficient 'comp.' An earlier try is 9 ♘b5 (instead of 9 ♘c4) and then 9 ... c6 10 ♘f4! ♘xf4 11 ♘c7+ ♔d8 12 ♘xa8 ♘g2+ 13 ♔d2. But Black can play more simply, 9 ... ♘xe3 10 fxe3 ♗d6. If a pawn is worth a little trouble, it's very little here. Many Happy Returns Masters love to grab material - while looking for a favorable way to give it back. They are seeking a kind of indirect trade in which they profit by eliminating weaknesses, gaining time or benefiting in some other way. White has an advantage in space on both wings. With his last move he traded pawns on a5. His goal was to create a pin on the newly-opened file with 1 ♖ab1. Black surprised him with 1 ... ♗xa3!, which he said was "an essential defensive move." On the one hand, he removed the bishop from the danger of f4-f5-f6!. On the other, he prepared to neutralize the queenside pressure with ... ♗b4!. White found nothing better than 2 f5. He eyed 3 f6 gxf6 4 ♗xh6. But 2 ... ♗b4! safeguarded both wings. After 3 ♗xb4 axb4 4 ♖xb4 ♕c7 Black's queen rook is in the game, he threatens ... ♕c5+ and the pawn at c4 is a potential target. White tried 5 ♔h1 and Black was able to win the e-pawn safely, 5 ... exf5 6 ♗xf5 ♗c6 7 ♕d2 (7 ♕c3 ♖a5) ♕xe5. He eventually won. It's usually easier to return a sacrificed pawn in a positional middlegame like that than in a largely tactical one. In attacking situations you're more likely to see a successful return of a sacrificed minor piece. A classic example of that was: Ahues - Carls, Hamburg 1921
1 e4 c6 2 d4 d5 3 exd5 cxd5 4 ♘f3 ♘c6 5 c3 e6 6 ♗d3 ♗d6 7 O-O ♘ge7 8 ♕e2 ♘g6
9 ♘g5 ♘ce7 10 f4 O-O 11 ♕h5 h6 12 ♘xf7 ♔xf7 13 g4 White threatens to regain his knight with 14 f5. Black can avoid that with 13 ... ♔f6, a move only a computer could love. White's calculation was accurate as far as that goes. But he assumed that ripping open the kingside must favor him. Black showed the flaw in his thinking with 13 ... ♕e8! and then 14 f5 exf5 15 gxf5 ♗xf5! 16 ♗xf5 ♔g8!. Black's king is safe and he is ready to seize the attack with 17 ... ♘xf5 18 ♖xf5 ♕e1+. White's best hope may have been 17 ♗e6+ ♔h7 18 ♖xf8. Then Black may like the solid endgame of 18 ... ♘xf8 19 ♕xe8 ♖xe8. But 18 ... ♕xf8 is more promising (19 ♗xd5? ♘xd5 20 ♕xd5 ♖e8 and the attack has changed hands). Instead, White chose to keep his bishop on the b1-h7 diagonal with 17 ♗d3? ♖xf1+ 18 ♔xf1?. After 18 ... ♕f7+ 19 ♔g1 ♖f8: It's easy to predict what happened to White's undermanned king position: 20 ♗e3 ♗f4! 21 ♕e2 ♕f6 22 ♔h1 ♕g5 23 ♗g1 ♗c1! 24 ♗xg6 ♘xg6 White resigned (25 ♘a3 ♘f4). A more subtle case was the next game. Black had a choice of being up the Exchange or one, two - or zero - pawns. The best option, it turned out, was the last. Olafsson - Tal, Bled-Zagreb-Belgrade 1959
1 e4 c5 2 ♘f3 e6 3 d4 cxd4 4 ♘xd4 a6 5 c4 ♘f6 6 ♘c3 ♗b4 7 ♗d3 ♘c6 8 ♘e2 ♕c7
9 O-O ♘e5 10 f4! ♘xc4 11 ♔h1 ♗e7 12 b3 ♘b6 13 e5 ♘fd5 14 ♘e4! f5 15 exf6 ♘xf6
16 ♘2g3 ♘bd5 17 ♗b2! Black saw that 17 ... ♘e3 would not win the Exchange because of 18 ♕c1!. Would White have enough compensation for the sacrificed pawn in the 18 ... ♕xc1 19 ♖fxc1 endgame? Both players believed so because of Black's difficulty developing his queenside and the prospect of ♗e5-d6. Black also had to consider taking a second pawn, 17... ♘xf4. This time it's clearly too risky because of 18 ♖c1 followed by a capture on f6. For example, 18 ... ♕b8 19 ♗xf6! ♗xf6?? loses to the fork, 20 ♖xc8+! ♕xc8 21 ♘d6+. Black would have to retake 19 ... gxf6 but his prospects are dim after 20 ♘h5. Instead, Black went for 17... O-O! 18 ♖c1 ♕d8!, reinforcing f6. The game went 19 ♕e2 b5 20 ♘xf6+ (better was 20 f5!). Black saw through 20 ... ♘xf6? 21 ♗xf6! and 22 ♕e4, hitting h7 and a8. His next big test came after 20 ... ♗xf6 21 ♕h5. Black - who was, after all, Mikhail Tal - didn't have to spend a lot of time to realize that 21... g6 would invite 22 ♗xg6! hxg6 23 ♕xg6+ ♔h8 and then 24 ♘f5! followed by ♖f3! with a deadly attack. The other way to stop 22 ♗xh7+ is 21... h6. Then 22 ♕g6 isn't convinc ing because there is no mate after 22 ... ♗xb2. But White has alternatives such as 22 ♗a3 and 23 ♗e5! (23 ... d6? 24 ♕g6 works now because of 24 ... dxe5 25 fxe5 ♗xe5?? 26 ♕h7 mate). He seemed to have plenty of play for the sacrificed pawn. When there seems to be few alternatives - and all of them are poor - the defender has to broaden his choice. Black must remember that he's a pawn up. That made 21 ... g6 22 ♗xg6 and 22 ... ♕e7! an option. This stops mate on h7 and threatens both 23 ... ♗xb2 and 23 ... hxg6. As a result Black can count on 23 ♗xf6 ♘xf6 and 24 ♕f3 being played. After he replied 24 ... ♖b8 he finally completed development with ... ♗b7. Play went 25 ♗d3 ♗b7 26 ♕e2 ♔h8 27 ♖ce1 ♖be8. Chances would have been equal - no more - after 28 ♘e4. But White thought he was still better and so he chose 28 ♘f5? ♕b4 29 ♕b2 because this was the only way to maintain the initiative (based on 29 ... exf5? 30 ♖xe8). But he underestimated 29 ... ♖c8!. Once again, pieces that were well-placed for attack are clumsy when the attack is over. Black threatens to take the knight. After 30 ♘g3 ♕c3! an endgame would have favored him. Play went 31 ♕e2 ♖g8 32 ♘e4 ♕d4! and Black had turned the tables (33 ♘xf6 ♖xg2!). He won the ending after 33 ♘g5 ♖g7! 34 ♕e3 ♕xe3. Post-Sacrifice The period immediately after a sacrifice is typically the most delicate time for the defender. His opponent has likely calculated a few moves further than him. The situation is almost certain to be one of the easier-for-the-attacker-to-play type. The defender has to set priorities: No. 1 is stopping immediate threats and No. 2 is anticipating longer-term threats. Lombardy - Keres, Mar del Plata 1957
1 d4 ♘f6 2 c4 e6 3 ♘c3 ♗b4 4 e3 b6 5 ♘e2 ♗a6 6 a3 ♗e7 7 ♘f4 d5 8 cxd5 ♗xf1 9
dxe6!? ♗a6 10 exf7+ ♔xf7 White has two pawns for his bishop and Black's king is vulnerable. White need not try to exploit this immediately, with 11 ♕b3+ ♔e8 12 ♘e6. That gains a third pawn but 12 ... ♕d7 13 ♘xg7+ ♔d8 would allow Black to coordinate his forces (e.g. 14 ♘e6+ ♔c8 15 ♗d2 ♘c6 and ... ♘a5). White chose the superior 11 e4!. If he can play e4-e5, then ♕b3+ will have much more punch. White can also just fortify his center with ♗e3, O-O, ♕b3+, ♖ad1 and ♖fe1 while Black is struggling to develop. Black realized he had to put immediate pressure on the White pawns with 11 ... c5!. One of his ideas was to answer 12 e5 with 12 ... ♕xd4! 13 exf6 ♗xf6, returning material advantageously. Another point is that 12 d5 ♘bd7 halts White's progress after 13 d6 ♗f8 or 13 ♘e6 ♕b8 14 f4 ♖e8 and ... ♘f8. White tried to keep control of the situation after 11 ... c5 with 12 ♗e3 ♘c6 13 ♕b3+!. To avoid a powerful 14 ♕a4, Black had to release pressure on the center, 13 ... c4. Then 14 ♕d1 prepared e4-e5! once again. But Black responded with 14 ... ♗d6!, threatening 15 ... ♗xf4. White had nothing better than 15 e5 ♘xe5 16 dxe5 ♗xe5. The give-back strategy has paid big dividends. Black was soon better after 17 ♘fd5 ♖e8 18 ♘xf6 ♕xf6 and won a long endgame. Consolidating The defender's task doesn't end when he survives a sacrificial attack. The position is likely to remain messy for several moves. His pieces need to be reorganized before he can make use of a material advantage. We call this process consolidating. Botvinnik - Stein, Moscow 1964
1 c4 g6 2 ♘c3 ♗g7 3 d4 ♘f6 4 e4 O-O 5 ♗e3 d6 6 f3 b6 7 ♗d3 ♗b7 8 ♘ge2 c5 9 O-O
♘c6 10 ♗c2 e5 11 dxe5 dxe5 12 ♗g5 h6 13 ♗h4 ♘d4 14 ♘d5 ♘xd5?! 15 ♗xd8 ♘e3 16 ♕d3! ♖axd8 17 ♕xe3! ♘xc2 18 ♕c3 ♘xa1 19 ♖xa1 ♗a6 Black's queen sacrifice has yielded only a rook and a bishop. But he has the two bishops, control of the d-file and chances for play with ... f5. White's winning plan is not clear, as 20 b3 h5 and ... ♗h6 followed by ... ♖d2 or ... ♗d2 (21 ♕e3 ♔h7) indicates. White began with 20 ♕c2!, with three ideas. He wants to drive the bishop back with ♕a4. He cleared c3 so his knight can reach d5 and ne gate the power of Black's rooks. And he creates the option of trading rooks with ♖d1. Black prepared to get his KB into the game with 20 ... h5. White didn't like 21 ♖d1? ♖xd1+ 22 ♕xd1 ♗xc4 and chose 21 ♕a4. His best winning chance lies in a creating a passed pawn. That's why Black played 21 ... ♖d2! and invited 22 ♕xa6 ♖xe2 23 ♕xa7? ♖xb2 (24 ♖d1 ♗h6 and ... ♗e3+-d4). White needed to reduce Black's counterplay before he thought about a passed pawn. He accomplished that with 22 ♘c3 ♗c8 23 ♖d1!. Black cannot double rooks (23 ... ♖fd8? 24 ♖xd2 ♖xd2 25 ♕e8+ and ♕xc8). He was near material equality after 23 ... ♖xb2. But his pieces were disorganized after 24 ♘d5!. This was better than 24 ♕xa7 because Black's rook is vulnerable (24 ... a6? 25 ♕a3! ♖c2 26 ♕b3 ♖e2 27 ♔f1! and wins). Black tried 24 ... ♗e6, with the idea of 25 ♕a3 ♗xd5 26 ♕xb2 ♗xc4. Then his two bishops and two extra pawns offer surprising resistance. White chose the more accurate 25 ♕xa7 ♗xd5 26 exd5! to obtain a passed pawn. There was more than one way to win after 26 ... b5 and White decided to run his pawn, 27 d6 bxc4 28 d7. Black's c-pawns offered counterplay with 28 ... c3 so White chose 29 ♕xc5 c2 30 ♖c1 ♖d8 31 ♖xc2 (so that 31 ... ♖xc2 32 ♕xc2 ♖xd7 33 ♕c8+). Black made one last try, 31 ... ♗f8, hoping for 32 ♕c6 ♖xc2 33 ♕xc2 ♖xd7. But White finished off with 32 ♕c8! ♖b8 33 ♕xb8! (33 ... ♖xb8 34 ♖c8). When your material edge is relatively minor, such as an extra pawn (or the Exchange), there is a strong temptation to make it grow, by grabbing a second pawn. Then you're certain you have enough material to win. But king safety and limiting enemy pieces usually takes the priority. Tal - Trifunovic, Havana 1963
1 e4 e5 2 f4 d5 3 exd5 e4 4 d3 ♘f6 5 dxe4 ♘xe4 6 ♗e3 ♕h4+ 7 g3 ♘xg3 8 hxg3?
♕xh1 9 ♕e2 White is preparing a discovered check (or 9 ... ♗e7 10 ♗c5!) as well as the quick development (♘c3, O-O-O, ♗g2). Can Black safely take the d-pawn? Yes. A computer might be happy with 9 ... ♕xd5 10 ♘c3 ♕d8 and 11 ♖d1 ♗d6 12 ♗d4+ ♔f8. Black chose the practical 9 ... ♗b4+!. On 10 ♘c3 he can castle safely, and his extra Exchange must be enough to win. White replied 10 c3 instead. But Black's finesse had denied him the best square for his knight. Black retreated the bishop to a protected square, 10 ... ♗d6. Then came 11 ♗g2, which sets a simple trap (11... ♕h2? 12 ♘f3 ♕xg3+?? 13 ♗f2+). Play went 11 ... ♕h6 12 ♗d4+ ♔d8. The Black king is perfectly safe on d8 since the d-file is blocked. Black's consolidation will involve: (1) Playing ... ♖e8(+) to disorient White's pieces, (2) Mobilizing his queenside pieces with... ♗g4 and ... ♘d7, and (3) Taking squares away from White bishop and (after ♘f3) his knight with ... f6!. Play went 13 ♘f3 ♗g4! 14 ♕f2 ♖e8+ 15 ♔f1 ♘d7 16 ♘bd2 ♕g6 17 ♔g1. White needs a new plan. Among the options: a well-prepared ♘e5, a push of the c-pawn to c5 and c6, and an attack on the queenside pawns from ♘c4-a5 and perhaps ♗xa7 (The immediate ♗xa7 fails to ... ♖xa7 and ... ♗c5+). Black ruled out the first idea with 17 ... f6!. He was ready to thwart oth ers with 18 ... ♕c2. And he could meet 18 ♘c4 with 18 ... ♕d3! followed by ... ♖e2. Then his initiative would be the more forceful one (19 ♘e3 ♗xf3 20 ♗xf3 ♗c5 21 ♖d1 ♕xe3! is a winning liquidation). White fell back on the c3-c4-c5 idea with 18 ♖c1, But Black countered with 18 ... b6 19 b4 a5! and then 20 ♘h4 ♕d3 21 ♘df3 ♖e2. The rest was: 22 ♕f1 axb4 23 ♖d1 ♖xg2+! 24 ♔xg2 ♖xa2+ 25 ♔g1 ♕xf1+ 26 ♔xf1 g5! White resigns. More complex post-sack situations occur when the defender risks not having enough pawns left to win.
