White's target is h7 but 1 ♖h3 f5 defends easily. Black might even be bet ter after 2 exf6 ♖xf6. White tried to keep matters moving with 1 ♗f5. Of course, 1... gxf5 is punished by 2 ♖g3+. But the main point is that White can shoot for 2 e6 and 2 ♖h3 (2 ... f6 3 ♗xg6). Black brought in a reinforcement, 1 ... ♗c8!. The attack would again be over following 2 ♖h3 f6 3 exf6 ♖xf6 (or 3 ♗xg6? ♗xh3). But White went for broke with 2 e6?, based on 2 ... ♗xe6 3 ♖ae1. The game went 2 ... fxe6 3 ♗xg6 and 3 ... hxg6 4 ♕xg6+ ♕g7 5 ♕h5. He was betting that Black would have no defense to ♖g3(+). But he was surprised by 5 ... ♖f5! 6 ♕h4 ♕f6!, not 6 ... ♖g5? 7 ♖g3! ♖xg3 8 ♕xd8+!. The game wasn't over. Black needed to consolidate after 7 ♖g3+ ♔f8 8 ♕h7 Consolidating is usually a slow process that consists of coordinating your pieces, getting your king to a safer place and offering trades - while meeting end-of-attack threats and retaining enough pawns to win. Here Black must stop ♖g8 mate - but not with 8 ... ♖g5? 9 ♕xa7! ♖xg3 10 fxg3!. That would give White chances for ♖f1 and ♕xb6. Black would be running out of winning pawns. He chose 8 ... ♕f7! and invited 9 ♕h8+ ♔e7 10 ♕h4+ ♔d7 because his king would be getting out of the danger zone. He was happier still when White played 9 ♕h6+ ♔e8 10 ♖g7 and allowed 10 ... ♖h5!, which forced an exchange of queens. True, White got a second pawn for his missing bishop, 11 ♖xf7 ♖xh6 12 ♖xa7. But with queens off the board, Black could coordinate his pieces more easily, 12 ... ♗d7 13 f4 ♖c8, and protected one of the pawns he needed to win, 13 ♖b1 b5!. Bad bishops defend good pawns. White's two extra pawns are slow (14 ♖b3 ♖c4
15 g3 ♔e7 16 h4 ♖h8 and ... ♖hc8 wins, for example).
He made a final gamble, 14 a4 bxa4 15 ♖bb7, which Black handled with 15 ... ♖h7 16 ♖xa4 ♗xa4! 17 ♖xh7 ♖xc3. White still had a pawn for a bishop, and his rook was on the seventh rank. But his pawns are no threat to queen. The game ended with 18 h4 ♗c2! 19 ♖a7 ♗e4 20 ♔h2 ♖c2 21 ♖g7 d4 22 h5 d3 23 h6 d2 White resigns. Brilliancy prizes are rarely awarded for refuting a sacrifice. The de fender has to content himself with putting points on a scoretable. And that should be satisfaction enough. Quiz White has just played ♘g3-f5. How should Black respond? Black threatens to seize the initiative with 1... ♖e2. He can punish 1 ♖e3 with
1... ♗f4!.
With that in mind he chose 1 ♗xg7 What should Black do? Chapter Eight: Prophylaxis Prophylaxis is the most mysterious realm of defense: It typically involves quiet, 'little' moves that don't seem to do much. But when you consider what they stop you appreciate how powerful they can be. This looks like a textbook illustration of a favorable isolated d-pawn middlegame. White owns the c-file and can take his time piling up on the d5-pawn. He threatens 2 ♘f4 followed by ♘xg6 or ♘xd5/♕xe4. Everything is falling into place for him except ... Except that Black's last move was ... ♘f5, which prepared a sacrifice on e3. If White allows 1 ... ♘xe3 2 fxe3 ♖xe3 Black would win with ... ♖xf3 or another killing discovered check. But White's reply changed the position powerfully - even though it was just 1 ♔h1!. Now a sacrifice on e3 would be harmless. And Black would gain nothing from 1 ... ♘h4 2 ♕g3 since a trade of queens magnifies the weakness of the d- pawn. Black chose 1 ... ♕g5 but the reply 2 ♖d1 prepared 3 ♖xd5! ♖xd5 4 ♕xe4. And after 2 ... ♘e7. White increased the pressure with 3 ♖cd2. None of White's last three moves looked strong. But by removing the threat of ... ♘xe3 White made the d-pawn what matters most in the position. Black can't defend it for long in face of ♘f4 or ♕g3 and a trade of queens. He tried 3 ... ♗a5 4 ♖d3 ♘g6 but after 5 ♘g3 he realized that even 5 ... ♖e5 (6 ♗xe5? ♘xe5) would fail in view of 6 e4!. He resigned. Prophylaxis is difficult to appreciate because there is a natural human preference for taking action rather than taking cover. Given a choice between counterplay and prophylaxis, most players lean heavily towards the former. Here's how they can be wrong: This position arises in the Dragon Sicilian. White's last move, h2-h4, advertises his intent to open the kingside with h4-h5xg6, with or without the support of g2- g4. In similar positions, 1 ... b5 is the right idea. Black offers a pawn to open lines and mobilize heavy pieces quickly (2 ♘dxb5 ♕b8 3 ♘d4 ♖fc8 and 4 ♘b3 a5 followed by ... a4). If White doesn't take the b-pawn, Black gets in ... b4 before White can accomplish anything on the kingside. But here 1 ... b5? is dubious as 2 h5! b4 3 ♘d5 shows. White's attack is faster after 3 ... ♘xd5 4 exd5 ♕c7 5 g4, for example (5 ... ♖c8 6 hxg6 fxg6 7 ♕h2 h6 8 ♖d2). And 2 ... ♘xh5 3 g4 ♘f6 4 ♕h2 or 4 b3 has been shown to favor White. The right defense is 1 ... h5!. Black will now have time to activate his heavy pieces (with ♕b8 and ... ♖fc8) without a kingside distraction. The usual downside to moves like 1 ... h5 is that they trade one point of attack at h7 for a more exposed one at h5. But 2 g4, the obvious way to attack the apparent new weakness, fails to 2 ... hxg4! 3 h5 ♘xh5 4 fxg4 ♗xg4 (5 ♖dg1 ♕d7 6 ♖h4 e5). Started with Steinitz Prophylaxis is hardly new. It was recommended by Wilhelm Steinitz when he annotated this consultation game: The Black allies intended 1 ... g4 2 ♗e2 g3, blasting open the White king position. If Black gets in 3 hxg3 ♖xg3, he would win with ... ♕g5. White made a natural attempt, 1 h3, to keep lines closed. But this overlooked the strength of 1 ... g4! 2 hxg4 h5!. Black would win a piece after 3 gxh5 ♖xf3 and would crash through after 3 ♗e2 ♖xf2 or 3 ♕e1 hxg4 (also 3 ... ♖xf3) 4 ♗d1 g3. In the game, the White team tried to minimize the danger with 3 g5 ♖xg5 4 ♔h2. But the attack was decisive after 4 ... ♖h4+ 5 ♔g1 ♕f4! 6 ♖e1 ♖xg2+! (7 ♗xg2 ♕xf2 mate or 7 ♔xg2 ♕h2+ and mates). So was White just lost in the diagram? After all, 1 h3 g4 2 ♗xg4 would also have been bad in view of 2 ... ♖gxg4! 3 hxg4 ♖xf2 ( 4 ♖xf2 ♕xf2+ and mates). No, said Steinitz. White's only vulnerable target was f2 - and losing the f-pawn shouldn't be fatal. What killed him was allowing the checks or possible discovered checks after ... ♖xf2. Steinitz recommended 1 ♔h1! and analyzed 1 ... g4 2 ♗e2. Then 2 ... ♗xf2 can be met by 3 g3!. White is safe after 3 ... ♖xe4 4 ♔g2 or 3 ... ♕h6 4 ♕d3! (but not 4 gxf4? ♗g3). In fact, taking the f-pawn is second best. Black would have greater winning chances after 2 ... ♖xe4. But with bishops of opposite color a win is far off. Curiously, the key move was the same ♔h1, as in the first example of this chapter. It's surprising how often king-to-corner pays off. White prepares ♖ef1 followed by a capture on f7 that would allow his queen to create mating chances around g8. For example, 1 ... ♖c3 2 ♖xc3 ♕xc3 seems to help Black because it trades off an active White rook for a somewhat passive Black one. But then 3 ♖f1 would threaten 4 ♖xf7 ♖xf7 5 ♕xf7+ ♔h7 6 ♕f5+ and mates (6 ... ♔h8 7 ♕f8+ ♔h7 8 ♗g8+). Black's solution was 1 ... ♔h8!. He came to the realization, similar to Steinitz's, that a capture on f7 is only dangerous if it is followed by checks. After the king move, 2 ♖xf7 ♖xf7 3 ♗xf7 lacks punch and Black would win with the 3 ... ♕f2! double attack. Better, but still not good, is 2 ♗xf7 because of 2 ... ♕xe4! when White has the more vulnerable king (3 ♖xe4 ♖c1+). Instead, White played 2 ♖ef1 and found his pieces in a mess after 2 ... ♕e2!. There were no tactics to exploit on f7 so play went 3 h4 ♗f4 and since 4 g3? is foiled by 4 ... ♗xg3 White tried 4 ♕g4 ♖c2. But he had no threats while Black's winning ideas include ... ♖d2-d1. White tried 5 h5 ♖d2 6 ♖xf4 exf4 but soon resigned. And her downfall began with 1 ... ♔h8!. Middlegame Fortress Creating an impregnable fortress is a basic defensive technique in the endgame, as we'll see in Chapter Ten. But prophylaxis can help build a middlegame version of it by ruling out the most aggressive enemy plans. With the center blocked, White turns his attention to the wings. Fortunately, he has a good plan on either one. The first is a queenside break with b2-b4. This would require minimal preparation (♕d2 and ♖ab1, for example). The second plan is g2-g4. It requires more buildup (♕c2, ♘f2, ♔h1, ♖g1). But it offers a bigger payoff by targeting the Black king as well as the e4-pawn. Yet Black was able to foil the first plan and make the second more difficult with one move, 1 ... ♗xc3!. After 2 bxc3 ♘f6: Clearly b2-b4 is impossible and White has no exploitable queenside target. On the other wing, Black can pile up heavy pieces on the g-file and make g2-g4 difficult, if not impossible. Play went 3 a4 ♔h8 4 ♘f2 ♖g8 5 ♔h1 ♕e8 6 ♖g1 ♕g6. If White had tried to hurry the g-pawn push with 7 h3 Black could simply block him with 7 ... ♕g3. White needed several more moves. The result was 7 ♕d2 ♗d7 8 g3 ♖ae8 9 a5 ♖e7! 10 ♖ab1 ♗c8 11 ♖g2 ♖eg7 12 ♖bg1 ♘ce8 13 h3 h5. White cannot increase his support for g3-g4 beyond ♕d1 (which isn't enough). Black had built an iron-clad fortress. The players agreed to a draw here. Prophylaxis and Counterplay Aron Nimzovich elaborated on the uses of prophylaxis in the 1920s. However, the emphasis then and for the decades to come was on safety for the sake of safety. It was the ounce of prevention to avoid needing a pound of cure. Prophylaxis was considered an alternative to counterplay. New Defense changes the focus: Prophylaxis can be the necessary preparation for counterplay. Anand - Ivanchuk, Leon 2008
1 e4 c5 2 ♘f3 e6 3 d4 cxd4 4 ♘xd4 ♘c6 5 ♘c3 ♕c7 6 ♗e2 a6 7 O-O ♘f6 8 ♗e3
♗e7 9 f4 d6 10 ♕e1 O-O 11 ♕g3 ♘xd4 12 ♗xd4 b5 13 a3 ♗b7 14 ♔h1 ♖ad8 15 ♖ae1 ♖d7 16 ♗d3 ♖e8 17 f5 e5 18 ♗e3 White's 13th move stopped ... b4. His 14th move anticipated lines in which the center is opened and Black delivers a key check on the open g1-a7 diagonal. These are common prophylactic moves in the Sicilian Defense. Black also took a prophylactic step, ... ♖ad8 to deter e4-e5. For example, 15 e5 dxe5 16 fxe5? ♖xd4. In the diagram, he played 18 ... ♔h8!. That prevented ♗h6. But it also prepared ... d5. The push wouldn't work immediately, 18 ... d5? 19 exd5 ♘xd5? 20 ♘xd5 ♗xd5 21 f6 or 21 ♗h6 ♗f6 22 ♗xg7 ♗xg7 23 f6. White tried to exploit the king's new position with 19 ♗g5, hoping for ♕h4, ♖e3-h3 and then ♗xf6/♕xh7 mate. For example, 19 ... ♗c6 20 ♕h4 ♕b7 readies 21 ... a5 and 22 ... b4. But 22 ♖e3 makes 23 ♖h3 strong. Black was happy to trade dark-squared bishops, 19 ... ♘h5! 20 ♕h4 ♗xg5 21 ♕xg5 ♘f6, because it put him on the verge of counterplay with ... d5 (or ... a5 and ... b4 first). Play went 22 ♖f3 ♕c5 23 ♖g3 ♖g8. The kingside is secure and Black, with a powerfully placed queen, is at least equal. After 24 ♖h3 he could have broken in the center with 24 ... d5 25 exd5 ♗xd5 (not 25... ♘xd5?? 26 ♖xh7+! ♔xh7 27 ♕h5 mate). He preferred 24 ... b4 25 axb4 ♕xb4 26 ♖b1 ♖c7 and won after ... d5. But what would happen in a similar position if there is no prospect of counterplay? The defender can wait with prophylactic measures: It's tempting to open the a-file with ... a5 and a trade of pawns on b4. But Black doesn't have much play on the file then after ... ♖a8 - and he has made his b5- pawn a potential target. Even though there was no immediate danger of ♘f6+, Black took the precaution of 1 ... ♔h8. After 2 c3 he added 2 ... ♖g8!. With his rook defending g7 he can decide, after ♘d6, whether to play ... ♗xd6. Then exd6 would threaten the Black queen but not ♕xg7 mate. After 3 ♖d3 Black decided to anticipate ♘d6 in another way, with the curious- looking 3 ... ♖cf8. This rules out ♘xf7+. 4 ♔h1 ♕b7. Black was not threatening anything. His waiting was rewarded by 5 ♕g4?. White prepared 6 ♗d1 followed by ♖h3 and ♗c2. But now the pent-up power of Black's kingside pieces made 5 ... ♘xe5! 6 ♗xe5 f5 work. White's position collapsed in a few moves, 7 ♘c5 fxg4 8 ♘xb7 gxf3 9 ♘a5 fxg2+ 10 ♔g1 ♗d5! 11 ♖xd5!? exd5 12 ♔xg2 ♗f6, and he resigned. Anticipation Prophylaxis is basically the anticipating of threats before they are threats. One reason that Tigran Petrosian was such a great defender is that he visualized threats before they occurred to his opponents. Bisguier - Petrosian, USA-USSR match 1954
1 d4 ♘f6 2 c4 c5 3 ♘f3 cxd4 4 ♘xd4 ♘c6 5 ♘c3 e6 6 g3 ♗c5 7 ♘b3 ♗e7 8 ♗g2 0-
0 9 O-O d6 10 e4 ♘e5 11 ♕e2 ♕c7 12 ♘d2 a6 13 b3 b5 14 f4 ♘c6 15 ♗b2 White can seek play in the center, with the aim of e4-e5, or on the kingside with g3-g4-g5 followed by f4-f5-f6. Black would gain little from 15 ... bxc4 16 ♘xc4 because then White can favorably turn his sights to the other wing, with ♖ac1. With that in mind, Black chose 15 ... b4!. If the attacked knight goes to a4 it would be out of play for quite some time. White preferred 16 ♘d1. Then 16 ... a5 17 ♘e3 a4 allowed Black to open the a-file, 18 ♖ab1 axb3 19 axb3 ♖a2. But his queenside initiative had temporarily come to an end. White began kingside operations with 20 g4. Black anticipated him with 20 ... ♘d7!. He can reorganize his kingside with ... ♖e8 and ... ♗f8 well before they are necessary. For example, 21 g5 could be met by 21 ... ♘c5 22 h4 ♖e8 23 f5 ♘e5 24 f6 ♗f8, for example, with a rock-solid position. He preferred 21 ... ♖e8 and then 22 ♔h1 ♘c5 23 h4 ♕d8. What is the queen doing on d8? It is there to prevent a pawn push to f6. But it also anticipates that he will strike in the center with ... e5! and doesn't want to allow ♘d5 with tempo. White didn't like 24 f5 ♘e5 (25 ♗xe5 dxe5 26 ♖fd1 ♕d4). He switched to a new plan, the preparation of g5-g6 by means of ♖f3-g3 and h4-h5. 24 ♖f3 ♗f8 25 ♖g3. Thanks to his preparations Black was ready for 25 ... e5! 26 f5 ♘d4!. White would be losing after 27 ♗xd4 exd4 28 ♘d5 ♗xf5 or 27 ♕d1 ♘d3!. He gave up a pawn under better circumstances, 27 ♕f1 ♘dxb3 28 ♘xb3 ♘xb3 29 ♕e1. He would have gotten some play after 29 ... ♕a5 30 ♘d5. But Black converted the extra pawn to piece activity: 29 ... ♘c5! 30 ♕xb4 ♗b7 31 ♘d5 ♖a4 32 ♕d2 (or 32 ♕c3 ♗a6 33 ♘e3 ♕b8 and ... ♖b4). He won soon after 32 ... ♗xd5! 33 ♕xd5 ♖b4! 34 ♗f3 ♕a8 35 ♕d2 ♕b7 36 ♖g2 ♖b8. As we've seen before, White's aggressively placed pieces were poor defenders when his threats or initiative ended. Here's another case. White shifted his knights to the kingside and prepared ♖g3, which would threaten ♘f6+ or ♘xh6+. If it were his move, 1 ♖g3 would favor him after 1 ... ♗xg4 2 ♕xg4 g6 3 f4 and 1 ... ♘g6 2 ♘xg6. But it just takes two precautions, 1 ... ♔h7! followed by ... ♘g8!, to defang the dangers. After 2 ♖g3 ♘g8 White discovered that his pieces were misplaced: Black threatens ... ♕xh4, and the natural 3 ♘f5? allows a knight to be trapped after 3 ... g6! 4 ♘fe3 h5!. Winning a piece that way may seem too risky. But here, because of Black's first two moves, it is perfectly safe. Instead, White retreated 3 ♘f3. Black seized the initiative with 3 ... ♗xg4! 4 ♖xg4 ♘f6 5 ♖h4 ♔g8 6 ♖h3 d5!. Now the biggest difference between White and Black is that the rook is offside at h3. As a result, White can't easily deal with the opening of the center. He made one last bid for attack, 7 ♕e2 ♕d7 8 ♘h4 dxe4 9 dxe4 ♖ad8 10 ♘f5. Then he threatened 11 ♖g3 and prepared 12 ♕f3 followed by ♘xh6+. But Black's pieces proved superior after 10 ... ♕d2! 11 ♕f3 ♖e6!. His rook defended f6 and his queen watched h6. Black headed to a winning endgame after 12 ♖g3 g6 13 ♖f1 ♕f4! (not 13 ... ♕xb2 14 ♕e3!). White still had tricks, 14 ♖d1 ♖ee8 15 ♘e7+. But they were foiled by 15 ... ♔g7 16 ♘d5 ♕xf3 and he lost the endgame after 17 ♖xf3 c6 18 ♘e3 ♖xd1+ 19 ♘xd1 ♖d8 20 ♘e3 ♘xe4. Counter-Intuitive It is relatively easy to find aggressive, forcing moves. They readily occur to the improving player. Prophylactic moves don't. To find them you have to put yourself in your opponent's chair and see what he wants to do. White has more space and more active pieces. But he needs one more piece to make things happen on the kingside. The only one not helping out is the rook at a1. However, when White thinks about 1 O-O-O he realizes the danger from 1 ... b4!. Then Black would have the target he desperately needs. By thinking prophylactically, White found 1 ♔e2!, He had safeguarded his king and prepared ♖ah1-h8 in the safest manner. 1 ... ♖c8 2 ♖ah1! ♘xd3 3 ♕xd3 ♖c4? 4 ♖h8!. White's heavy pieces invaded - 4 ... ♖xh8 5 ♖xh8+ ♔e7 6 ♕h7! with a threat of ♕h4+ and mates. The end was 6 ... ♗e8 7 ♕g8 ♖c8 8 f5! resigns in view of fxe6, e.g. 8 ... exf5 9 ♕f8+ ♔d7 10 e6+ ♔c7 11 ♕e7+ or 10 ... fxe6 11 ♖h7+. Another reason prophylaxis is counter-intuitive is that it seems to lose time: Jakovenko - Karjakin, Sochi 2012 1 ♘f3 ♘f6 2 c4 b6 3 g3 c5 4 ♗g2 ♗b7 5 O-O g6 6 ♘c3 ♗g7 7 d4 cxd4 8 ♕xd4 d6 9 ♖d1 ♘bd7 10 ♗e3 ♖c8 11 ♖ac1 a6 12 b3 O-O 13 ♕h4 Black played 13 ... ♖c7 for two reasons. One is aggressive. He wants to play ... ♕a8 and ... ♖fc8 so that ... b5 will be strong. The other reason is defensive. He anticipated ♗h3, which would pin the d7-knight if the rook still stood at c8. The picture changed when White replied 14 g4. Black recalled how he played 14 ... ♖c8!? and another of the elite GMs in the tournament "looked at me like I was a complete idiot." Black's play was again prophylactic. He knew that g4-g5 might be coming and, after he answered it with ... ♘h5, he didn't want ♘d5 to attack the c7-rook. White replied 15 ♗h3. It might seem that Black has just wasted two tempi and White used the time to make good moves, ♗h3 and g3-g4. But those moves didn't look useful after Black shot back 15 ... b5! 16 cxb5 ♕a5 and threatened 17 ... ♖xc3 (and 18 ♗d2 ♖xc1 19 ♗xa5 ♖xd1+). If White didn't have his extra moves, 17 bxa6 would be fine. But here Black would have had excellent compensation for a pawn after 17 ... ♗xf3 18 exf3 ♘e5 or
18 g5 ♖xc3 19 exf3 ♘d5! (and 20 ♗xd7 ♘xe3 21 fxe3 ♖xc1 22 ♖xc1 ♕xa6).
The game went 17 ♗d2 ♗xf3 18 exf3 axb5 19 g5 b4! and Black had the better of the complications (20 gxf6 ♗xf6 21 ♕e4 bxc3 22 ♗xd7 ♖cd8 23 ♗xc3 ♗xc3 24 ♕xe7 d5). And a third reason that prophylactic moves are counter-intuitive is that they often seem to overprotect the already well-protected. Black chose 1 ... ♖e6, with ... ♕c7 and ... ♖ae8 in mind. But the e-file is closed. What would the rooks do on e6 and e8? Well, Black first had to deal with an immediate threat (2 ♘h6+ gxh6 3 ♗xf6). He also had to face a longer-term threat, ♖ad1 and d3-d4!, which could embarrass his queen at d8. The wisdom of his maneuvers was clear after 2 ♕g3 ♕c7 3 ♖ad1 ♖ae8 4 axb5 axb5. He was ready to play for an edge with ... d4 and/or ... c4. White continued with his plan, 5 d4?, perhaps expecting 5 ... ♘xe4 6 ♗xe4 dxe4 7 d5!, which favors him. But Black improved with 5 ... dxe4. 6 ♗xf6 ♖xf6 7 ♗xe4 ♗xe4 8 ♖xe4. White had overlooked 8 ... ♕c8! and the threat of ... ♖xf5. The f5-knight has no good retreat (9 ♘e3 ♖f4!). He would be losing after 9 ♘xg6 ♕xf5! or 9 ♕g4 ♘xh4 10 ♘xh4 ♕xg4 11 ♖xg4 exd4 12 cxd4 c4!. In the end he played 9 dxe5 and 9 ... ♖xf5 10 ♘xf5 ♕xf5 11 ♖de1. But the minor pieces prevailed after 11 ... ♖d8 12 ♕e3 c4 13 e6 fxe6 14 ♖xe6 ♗c5 15 ♖e8+ ♔f7. Sacrificial Prophylaxis Finally, it should be noted that not all prophylactic moves are quiet. Some are dramatic because they give up material. White has positional trumps, including two potential passed queenside pawns that could be decisive. But he has to deal with ... h4, which would blow open his king position. The short-term response, 1 ♕f2, seems adequate. But after 1 ... f5 Black's counterplay from 2 ... h4 3 gxh4 ♕h6 and ... f4, or from ... ♖f6-h6, is beginning to get serious. White found a long-term solution in 1 ♖f4! and 1 ... ♗xf4 2 gxf4. This keeps his kingside firm and makes his knight a powerhouse when it goes to d4. True, Black still had an h-pawn to push and did: 2 ... ♕g6 3 ♘d4 h4 4 ♔h1 h3. It appeared as if White's sacrifice had failed when 4 ♗f1 ♗g4 made ... ♗f3+ a threat. Yet White had another prophylactic measure, 5 ♔g1! followed by ♔f2. There followed 5 ... ♗e2+ 6 ♔f2 ♗xf1 7 ♖xf1 ♖fc8 8 ♖g1 ♕f6 9 ♕c3. White has all the play (♖g5, ♕b3, b4-b5). He eventually won three pawns and coasted to victory. You can defend many, if not most, positions without prophylaxis. But to become a complete defender, to get the most out of the wide variety of situations you'll find yourself in, it pays to learn what prophylaxis can do for you. Quiz Black's position looks dire in view of ♗xf6/♕xh7 mate. What can he do? White has been preparing e4-e5!. How can Black anticipate that? White's plan is ♗xf6 and g2-g4-g5. Do any ideas occur to you? Chapter Nine: You Against Tal Decades after his death, Mikhail Tal remains the iconic ideal of the all-out aggressive player, the attacker against whom all others can be measured. But his sacrifices didn't automatically succeed. Nor did his initiatives always translate into brilliancy prizes. He was beaten by good defense. Let's see what Tal's games can teach us about the limits of attack. In this chapter we'll put you in the chair of his opponents. Can you defend against Tal? Tal - Romanishin, Riga 1981
1 e4 c5 2 ♘f3 e6 3 d4 cxd4 4 ♘xd4 ♘c6 5 ♘c3 a6 6 ♗e2 ♘ge7 7 ♗e3 ♘xd4 8
♕xd4 b5 9 ♖d1 ♗b7 10 O-O ♘c6 11 ♕d2 ♗e7 12 ♗f4 ♖c8 13 ♖fe1 ♘a5 14 ♗d6! ♗xd6 15 ♕xd6 ♕c7 16 ♕a3 ♘c4 17 ♗xc4 ♕xc4 White met the threat of 18 ... b4 with his own threat, 18 ♘d5, of mate on e7. Do you accept the knight sacrifice or refuse? And if you refuse, how? Take a few minutes to figure out a response before reading on. The first thing you may have noticed is that after 18 ... exd5 19 exd5+ ♔d8 White has a major choice. He can grab a second pawn (or a third) with 20 ♕e7+ ♔c7 21 ♕xf7. But then his threats are dwindling and he will have a hard time making his compensation count. On the other hand, White can stop Black's king from escaping to c7 with either 20 ♕g3 or 20 d6 (with its threat of 21 ♕a5+). The outcome would be unclear. In the game, Black let Tal take the risks. He played 18 ... ♕c5!. That gave Tal a chance to be consistently aggressive - and get the worst of it with 19 b4? ♕xc2!. For example, 20 ♖c1 ♕d2 or 20 ♕g3 ♗xd5! 21 exd5 O-O or 21 ♕xg7 ♕c3!. Tal appreciated that the 19 ♕xc5 endgame would be fine for Black. To keep his attacking hopes alive he tried 19 ♕g3. He invited 19 ... exd5 20 ♕xg7! followed by exd5+. But once again Black was able to shift the burden back to White, with 19 ... ♗xd5!. He dared Tal to play 20 ♕xg7? ♕f8! 21 ♕xf8+ ♖xf8 because 22 exd5 ♖xc2 favors Black. Tal saw the light, recaptured 20 exd5 - and offered a draw. It was accepted because the position is balanced after 20 ... O-O 21 c3. Another way to deal with an attack-mad opponent is to take super-precautions. Don't give a Tal the chance to play like a Tal. Tal - Andersson, Las Palmas 1975
1 e4 c5 2 ♘f3 d6 3 d4 cxd4 4 ♘xd4 ♘f6 5 ♘c3 e6 6 ♗e2 a6 7 O-O ♗e7 8 f4 ♕c7 9
♗e3 O-O 10 ♕e1 ♘c6 11 ♕g3 ♘xd4 12 ♗xd4 b5 13 a3 ♗b7 14 ♖ae1 ♗c6 15 ♔h1 ♖ab8 16 e5! ♘e8 17 ♗d3 White would get a small positional edge from 17 ... b4 18 axb4 ♖xb4 19 ♘e2. But 18 ♕h3! is far more promising. Then 18 ... h6 19 ♘e4 prepares ♘f6+. And 18 ... g6 19 ♖e3! bxc3? 20 exd6 ♗xd6 21 ♕xh7+! ♔xh7 22 ♖h3+ is a mate. Black played 17 ... g6! (instead of 17 ... b4) so his knight can go to g7. He didn't fear 18 f5 exf5 19 ♗xf5 because of 19 ... dxe5 20 ♗xe5 ♗d6!. True, White has good alternatives to 18 f5, such as 18 ♘e4 or 18 exd6 ♗xd6 (18 ... ♘xd6? 19 f5! exf5 20 ♕e5! wins) 19 ♕h4. But the biggest drawback to 17 ... g6 is that it made h7 more vulnerable to ♕h3- h6/♖e3-h3. Tal went for the quick knockout with 18 ♕h3 and 18 ... ♘g7 19 ♕h6. White threatens to win immediately with 20 exd6 followed by ♕xg7 mate or dxc7. If that is thwarted, he also has 20 ♖e3 and ♖h3. What would you do as Black? Take a few minutes. The best defense turns out to be bunkering down with the highly cautious 19 ... f6! 20 ♖e3 ♖f7!. He protected g7 and prepared ... ♗f8 followed by ... ♘f5 or ... ♘h5. By adding this over-protection he could refute 21 ♖h3 ♘h5 22 ♖xh5? with 22 ... ♗f8!. The effect on Tal was crucial. He could have kept an edge (after 21 ♖h3 ♘h5) with 22 exf6! ♗xf6 23 ♗xf6 ♘xf6 24 ♗xg6! (24 ... ♖g7 25 ♘e4!). However, Tal was intrigued by a pretty move, 21 f5. It works wonderfully after 21 ... exf5 22 e6! ♖ff8 23 ♖h3 ♘h5 and now 24 ♖xf5!. Also winning is 21 ... fxe5 22 fxg6 ♖xf1+ 23 ♗xf1 hxg6 and now 24 ♗d3! exd4 25 ♖h3. But it doesn't matter how many pretty ways to lose there are if there is one line that successfully defends. Black found 21 ... ♘xf5!. This turned the tables. Play went 22 ♗xf5 exf5! 23 e6 ♖g7 24 ♘e2 and a draw was agreed - but 24 ... ♗e4! would have given Black strong winning chances. Choice of Weapons The defensive weapons we examined in Chapter Four occur over and over in Tal's games. Which weapon occurs to you in the next position? With five pieces aimed at the Black kingside, White looked for a way to sacrifice on h6 or g7. This time super-caution can be dangerous: If Black overprotects h6 with 1 ... ♘g8, for example, he allows 2 ♘xh6! ♘xh6 3 ♗xh6 gxh6? 4 ♕xf7+ or 3 ... ♕xh4 4 ♗g5 and 3 ... ♔xh6 4 ♘f5+. Black's solution was long-distance reinforcement, 1 ... ♖a6!. The rook protects the f6-knight in key lines. It also frees a8 for Black's queen, which can take aim at e4 and provide counterplay. White pulled the trigger with 2 ♘xh6 gxh6 3 ♘f5. His idea was 4 ♘xh6 ♗xh6 5 ♕f5+ ♔h8 6 ♗xh6 followed by ♗g7+ (if allowed) or ♗g5. That might win if he can also get his other rook or bishop into the game after f2-f4. Black carried out his regrouping with 3 ... ♕a8! and threatened 4 ... cxd3 5 ♗xd3 ♗xe4! to kill the attack. Play went 4 ♘xh6 ♗xh6 5 ♕f5+ ♔h8 6 ♗xh6 ♖g8 7 ♗g5. Now we can appreciate how the rook at a6 defends f6. But Black can't play 7 ... cxd3 8 ♗xd3 ♗xe4 because of 9 ♗xe4 ♕xe4 10 ♗xf6+! ♘xf6 11 ♖xg8+ ♔xg8 12 ♕c8+! and ♕xa6. Black could have over-protected further with 7 ... ♖g6 but White would pick up a third pawn for the piece after 8 ♗xf6+ (8 ... ♖axf6? 9 ♕xd7). And 7 ... ♖xg5 would give away his material edge. Black found 7 ... ♕e8!, which protects d7 and f7 and prepares to trade pieces with 8 ... ♘h7!. White tried 8 f4 ♘h7! 9 ♗h4 ♖xg3 10 ♗xg3. But Black's pieces took over the board, 10 ... ♖f6! 11 ♕g4 ♖g6 12 ♕h4 exf4 13 ♕xf4 f6!. He won after 14 ♗f2 cxd3 15 ♗xd3 ♘e5! 16 ♗f1 ♘g5 17 ♔h1 ♘xe4. White's weapons were good. Black's were better. Most Exciting Draw Tal's finest draw - and what's been called the most exciting draw ever played - featured a remarkable insight by the defender. His opponent had to figure out what was the indispensable member of Tal's attacking army and get rid of it. He passed the test. Can you? Both ... ♖xd3 and ... ♗xd1 are threatened. Tal blithely ignored both with 1 hxg6!. The first question Black must answer is whether he can allow gxf7+ or gxh7+. He figured out that 1 ... ♖xd3! was safe because 2 gxf7+ ♕xf7! wins for Black. And 2 gxh7+ ♔h8 3 ♖xd3 is simply met by 3 ... ♗e6. White recaptured 2 ♖xd3! and was ready to beat 2 ... ♖d8 with 3 gxh7+! ♔h8 4 ♘f7+!. Black found the correct 2 ... hxg6! and White revealed the point of his queen sacrifice with 3 ♖h7!. The real threat is 4 f3!, of all things. Then if the attacked bishop retreats to h5, White wins with 5 ♖d7 and 6 ♖dxf7. If, instead, the bishop goes the other way, say to e6, White has 5 ♖d2 and 6 ♖dh2 followed by mate on h8. The natural responses of a defender in a position like this are (a) panic and (b) confusion, followed by (c) trying to trade off one of the rooks. If (c) fails, he is left with (d), giving back some material. But 3 ... ♖d8? loses to the pretty 4 ♖xd8 ♕xd8 5 ♖h8+! ♔xh8 6 ♘xf7+ and ♘xd8. And the giveback option, 3 ... ♖e6 4 ♘xe6 ♗xe6, would allow 5 ♖g7+ and ♖d1-h1+ or 4 ... fxe6 5 ♖g7+ ♔h8 6 ♖dd7 ♕xf6 7 ♖gf7!. But there is also option (e). Black can look for a key element to White's attacking force and eliminate it. Can you spot it? Take a few minutes. Black realized that it was the pawn at f6. That told him he had to find a safe way (not 3 ... ♖e6?) to get rid of it. He found 3 ... ♖c8! 4 f3 ♖c6!. Tal saw that 5 fxg4 ♖xf6 would favor Black. He bailed out with 5 ♖xf7 and a draw was agreed soon after 5 ... ♕xf7 6 ♘xf7 ♔xf7 7 fxg4 ♔xf6. Over-Reaching Tal's ingenuity required defenders to thread their way through a ticket of traps, as Black did in the last example. The defender's reward for navigating his way to safety was that Tal occasionally over-reached. Tal - Bhend, Zurich 1959
1 d4 c5 2 d5 d6 3 e4 g6 4 ♘f3 ♗g7 5 ♗e2 ♘a6 6 O-O ♘c7 7 ♘c3 f5 8 ♗d3 fxe4 9
♘xe4 ♘h6 10 ♗f4 O-O 11 ♕d2 ♘f7 12 c4 b5? 13 ♖ae1 ♖b8 14 b3 e6 15 ♘fg5! ♘xg5 16 ♗xg5 ♕d7 17 ♘c3 b4 18 dxe6! ♘xe6 19 ♘d5 ♗b7 20 ♗e4 ♘xg5 21 ♕xg5 It's tempting to get rid of that dominating knight. But 21 ... ♗xd5? 22 ♗xd5+ ♔h8 23 ♖e7 helps White. What else should you consider as Black? In the absence of counterplay, prophylaxis, 21 ... ♔h8!, stands out. White was threatening 22 ♘e7+ ♔h8 23 ♗xg6 or 22 ♗xg6 hxg6 23 ♘e7+. Also, since ... ♗xd5 may be inevitable, Black wanted to make sure that ♗xd5 would not be check. For example, 22 ♖e3 could be met by 22 ... ♗xd5 23 ♗xd5 ♖be8!. Then with bishops of opposite color, Black's target at f2 is as least as vulnerable as White's at h7 (and 24 ♖h3 ♗d4 25 ♕xg6 fails to 25 ... ♕xh3!). Tal accepted the challenge and played 22 ♗xg6 anyway, because 22 ... hxg6 23 ♘e7! still wins (23 ... ♖f5 24 ♕h4+ ♖h5 25 ♘xg6+). Black's timely 21st move allowed him to answer 22 ♗xg6 with 22 ... ♗xd5!. The first point is that 23 cxd5 or 23 ♕xd5 allows Black to pocket a piece safely (23 ... hxg6). But the more subtle second point is that 23 ♕h5 also fails, to 23 ... ♗g8!. Did Tal just miss that retreat when he sacrificed the bishop? No, he calculated quite a bit further. This became apparent after 23 ♖e7!. Then 23 ... ♗f6? allows White to swap down to a favorable endgame (24 ♕xf6+ or 24 ♖xd7 ♗xg5 25 ♖xh7+ and 26 cxd5). Also 23 ... ♕c8?? fails to, among other moves, 24 ♗f5!. But 23 ... ♕d8! pins the e7-rook. Black can meet 24 ♗xh7? and the threat of ♕xg7 mate with 24 ... ♗f6! 25 ♕h6 ♕xe7 and wins (26 ♗e4+ ♔g8 27 ♗d5+ ♖f7). Black has passed three tests so far. But Tal posed a fourth with 24 ♖fe1!. Now on 24 ... ♗g8 White wins with 25 ♗xh7!. And against 24 ... ♖g8 he has 25 ♕h6! ♗xh6 26 ♖xh7 mate. Black had to be impressed by that. But remember that the rook at e7 is pinned. Take a few minutes to consider Black's options. He can't defend with 24 ... ♖b7 (25 ♖xb7?? ♕xg5) because White has 25 ♕h5 ♗g8 26 ♖xb7. Black found a way to exploit the pin: 24 ... ♗f7! so that 25 ♗xf7 ♖xf7. This prepares to kill the attack with 26 ... ♖xe7 27 ♖xe7 ♕f8. Note that 26 ♖e8+ ♕xe8 27 ♖xe8+ ♖xe8 would give Black more than enough material for the queen. In this calculation contest, Tal erred first. He could have held the position with the unlikely 25 ♗h5! because then 25 ... ♖b7? can be met by 26 ♗xf7 ♖xf7? 27 ♖e8+!. After 25 ♗h5 Black can draw - but just draw - with 25 ... ♗d4 26 ♕h6! ♗g7! 27 ♕g5 ♗d4. Instead, White played 25 ♗c2? and there followed 25 ... ♖b7! 26 h4 ♖xe7 27 ♖xe7. White's queen is protected now, so he threatens 28 ♖xf7 ♕xg5 29 ♖xf8+ or 28 ... ♖xf7 29 ♕xd8+. Black has to pass one final test. There are two defenses. The simplest is 27 ... ♗g6, again exploiting the pin and trading off another piece. This would require Black to win the endgame after 28 ♗xg6 hxg6 29 ♖xa7. Black preferred to think out 27 ... h6!. The attack ended after 28 ♕f5 ♗g8! and Black's extra piece won (29 ♕e4 d5 30 cxd5 ♕xd5 31 ♕g6 ♕d4 32 ♕g3 ♖f7!). Accept or Not? When trying to decide whether to accept a sacrifice it pays to recall that there are positions that are more difficult for the attacker to play than for the defender. That helped defeat Tal in this game. Polugayevsky - Tal, Moscow 1973
1 c4 g6 2 e4 e5 3 d4 ♘f6 4 ♘f3 ♗b4+ 5 ♗d2 ♗xd2+ 6 ♘bxd2 d6!? 7 dxe5 dxe5 8
♘xe5 ♕e7 9 f4 ♘bd7 10 ♘xd7 ♗xd7 11 e5 White grabbed the e-pawn because he saw that if Black tried to win a pawn back, 8 ... ♕d4, he could be punished by 9 ♘d3! ♘xe4 10 ♕e2!. The position in the diagram is risky for White - yet difficult to play as Black because he has three appealing alternatives, 11 ... g5, 11 ... ♘h5 and 11 ... O-O-O. The first one looks good after 12 fxg5 ♘g4!. But the simple 12 ♗e2 would force a knight retreat and send Black's initiative floundering. The ... g5 idea works much better after 11 ... ♘h5! 12 ♕f3 ♗c6 and 13 ♕f2 g5!. White could improve with 13 ♕g4 but 13 ... ♗d7 would repeat the position and 13 ... ♕c5 and ... ♕e3(+) might offer more than a draw. Tal chose 11 ... O-O-O and that could be considered his first error of the game, although a minor one. Take a moment here to imagine what you would play as White. His task is actually easier than Black's. In view of the possibility of 12 ... ♘h5, 12 ... ♖he8 and other dangerous ideas, White's reply was virtually forced. Did you find 12 ♗e2 ? Good. Then if Black retreats 12 ... ♘e8 White must stand well after 13 ♕b3 (13 ... ♗c6 14 ♗f3!). A bit trickier is 12 ... ♘h5! because 13 ♗xh5 gxh5 14 ♕xh5 ♖hg8 gives Black good play. But White doesn't need the second pawn and would have the upper hand after just 14 O-O!. Black again had the more difficult task and 12 ... ♗c6? was second best. It helped White by narrowing his choices. White saw he could either defend against ... ♗xg2 or take the knight on f6. He also saw that he would have a hard defense after 13 ♗f3 ♘e4 14 ♗xe4 ♗xe4 (15 O-O ♕b4). So he might as well devote his calculation to 13 exf6!. It wasn't hard to see that the best response would be 13 ... ♕e3!. White's moves are easy to find when you can rule out the losing alternatives (14 ♕b3? ♕xd2+; 14 ♘f1? ♖xd1+; 14 ♔f1 ♖xd2 and 14 ♕c1? ♖he8). That left 14 ♖f1! and then 14 ... ♗xg2 15 ♖f2. The threat of ♖xg2 gave White time to take a breath and study the position after 15 ... ♗h3. It should be obvious that White can only move his queen and QR. The queen moves would prepare ♘f1! while 16 ♖c1 would set up ♖c3. White opted for 16 ♕c1! - rather than 16 ♕c2 ♗f5 17 ♕c3 ♖d3. Then came 16 ... ♖he8 17 ♘f1! ♕e6 18 ♕c3. He had survived the initial crisis and met 18 ... ♗g4 with 19 ♘g3. Then 19 ... ♗xe2 20 ♖xe2 ♕g4 would win the f4-pawn. But with a knight to defend the kingside White can escape to safety with 21 ♖xe8 ♖xe8+ 22 ♔f2 ♕xf4+ 23 ♔g2 (23 ... ♖e3 24 ♖f1!). The last major challenge was posed by 19 ... h5! (instead of 19 ... ♗xe2). Once again White's choices are so limited that it helped him decide. He can quickly reject 20 ♕e5 ♕c6, for example. More attractive is 20 h4 but he might be losing that pawn after ... ♗xe2 and ... ♕g4. The choice is mainly between seeking king safety (20 ♔f1) and trading pieces (20 ♖d1 and ♖xd8+). Both policies have merits, since 20 ♔f1 h4 21 ♘e4! defends (21 ... ♕xe4 22 ♗xg4+). But White opted for 20 ♖d1 h4 21 ♖xd8+. After 21 ... ♖xd8 he could defend with 22 ♘f1 ♖e8 since 23 ♕e5! works this time. Black's choice was once again difficult and he preferred 21 ... ♔xd8. He apparently overlooked or underestimated 22 f5! which led to 22 ... gxf5 23 ♕d3+ ♔c8 24 ♘xf5. The knight was ready to kill the attack with ♘e7+ or ♘d4. Black tried 24 ... ♕xf6 but he resigned soon after 25 ♘e3! ♗xe2 26 ♖xe2 ♕g7 27 ♘d5. Give-Back Time Even Tal could be thrown off when an opponent gave sacrificed material back to him. Can you figure out how to do it effectively? Tal - Huebner, Leningrad 1973
1 e4 c5 2 ♘f3 e6 3 d4 cxd4 4 ♘xd4 a6 5 ♘c3 ♕c7 6 ♗e2 b5 7 a3 ♗b7 8 f4 ♘c6 9
♗e3 ♘xd4 10 ♕xd4 ♘e7 11 ♖d1 ♖d8 12 O-O ♘c6 13 ♕d2 ♗e7 14 ♕e1 d6 15 f5 O-O!
16 f6 ♗xf6 17 ♖xf6 gxf6
Black knew that an Exchange sacrifice on f6 was possible when he castled. But he appreciated that 15 ... ♘e5, for example, would be more dangerous. Then 16 fxe6 fxe6 17 ♕g3! ♗f6 18 ♗h5+ ♘f7 and now 19 ♖xf6! gxf6 20 ♕g7 would promise a winning attack. In the diagram it looks like White has all the weapons. But Black has an extra pawn and Exchange. He can give up some of his material surplus. For example, 18 ♖d3 ♘e5 19 ♗h6 prepares ♕h4. This looks dangerous in view of 19 ... ♔h8 20 ♕h4 ♕e7 21 ♖h3 followed by ♗xf8 or ♗g7+ (or 21 ... ♖g8 22 ♗f8!). But the knight reinforcement would arrive in time, 19 ... ♘g6! (20 ♖g3 ♕c5+ 21 ♔h1 ♔h8 with advantage). So instead of the immediate 18 ♖d3 Tal played 18 ♕h4 ♕e7 19 ♖d3. He set a delicious trap. This time the safety-minded 19 ... ♔h8?? loses to 20 ♗d4! ♘xd4 21 ♖h3!. Black found the superior 19 ... f5!. Of course, White declined to trade queens. But his 20 ♗g5 f6 21 ♗h6 was simply met by 21 ... fxe4!. White was so far behind in material that he had no alternative to 22 ♖g3+ ♔h8 23 ♗g7+ ♕xg7 24 ♖xg7 ♔xg7. Then material was roughly equal. But Black had the better chances, e.g. 25 ♕g4+ ♔h8 26 ♕xe6 ♘e5 and ... f5-f4 or 25 ♘xe4 ♘e5 26 ♘g3 ♔g8. He eventually won. One final defensive technique works best when facing a strong opponent: Look for the resource that even a good calculator might have overlooked when he first considered the sacrifice. Tal - Keres, Moscow 1959
1 e4 c6 2 d4 d5 3 ♘c3 dxe4 4 ♘xe4 ♗f5 5 ♘g3 ♗g6 6 ♘f3 ♘d7 7 h4 h6 8 ♗d3
♗xd3 9 ♕xd3 ♘gf6 10 ♗f4 ♕a5+ 11 c3 e6 12 O-O ♗e7 13 ♖fe1 O-O 14 ♘f5 ♖fe8 White can get a small edge with 15 ♘xe7+ and 16 ♘e5. But Tal wanted more. He tried 15 ♘xg7 ♔xg7 16 ♘e5. What could have convinced him that the knight sacrifice was sound? One answer is the threats he would get from 17 ♗xh6+! ♔xh6 18 ♘xf7+ ♔g7 19 ♖e5! (19 ... ♘xe5 20 ♘xe5 and ♕g6+ or 19 ... ♕c7 20 ♖g5+). He might also have thought in general terms: The knight on e5 keeps Black's queen from joining the kingside defense. After 16 ... ♘xe5 he can choose between 17 ♗xe5, with the idea of ♖e3-g3+ or -f3, and 17 ♖xe5. This is far from convincing, as 16 ... ♘xe5 17 ♗xe5 ♖g8 and ... ♔f8 indicates. But Black preferred 16 ... ♖h8, which has the advantage of protecting h6 and clearing an escape route for the king. Tal had an ingenious response, 17 ♕h3, with the idea of 18 ♘xf7! ♔xf7 19 ♕xe6+ and ♕xe7+ (or 19 ... ♔g6 20 ♖e3 and ♖g3+). White may have seen this far when he sacrificed the knight. In that case he would have examined 17 ... ♘xe5 and then 18 ♗xe5 followed by ♖e3-f3. But there's an alternative to 17 ... ♘xe5 and it's one that would have been very hard to visualize, let along calculate with certainty, when White was looking at 15 ♘xg7. Take a few minutes to look at this position and see if you can figure out what that might be. The answer Black came up with was 17 ... ♖h7!. Not only does it meet the threat - 18 ♘xf7? ♔xf7 19 ♕xe6+ ♔e8 (20 ♗d6 ♕d5) - but it does so in a way that White probably didn't foresee when the rook was at f8 and the king at g8 in the first diagram. White's attack began to run out of steam after 18 c4 ♘f8! 19 ♖ad1 ♖d8 20 ♗d2 ♕b6 21 ♗c3 ♔g8 and he was on the way to a loss after 22 ♖e3 ♘e8 23 ♖g3+ ♘g7 24 ♖dd3 f6 25 ♘g6 ♘xg6 26 ♖xg6 ♔f7. And now for some quiz positions drawn from Tal's games. Quiz The first defensive move that would occur to many players is 1 ... g6. Then White could try to win a good-N-vs-bad-B ending after 2 ♖c1. Is there better for Black? White, a pawn down, threatens ♖xh5. There are four ways to parry that. How many are good? White plans ♗xf6 and ♕g5 mate and also has ♖f1/♘f5 coming up. Is 1 ... fxg5 2 ♕xg5+ with a perpetual check the best Black has? White prepared ♖xh6+. For example on 1 ... ♗e2, he would obtain the edge with 2 ♖xh6+! gxh6 3 ♖f6 ♕c7+ 4 ♘c6!! (not 4 ♔d2 ♕a5+ 5 ♔xe2 ♕g5!). What else can Black do? Black is in check. What is the safe way out? Chapter Ten: Last Chance to Defend When you have a worse position and you're getting closer to an endgame, your defensive resources begin to dwindle. This means you should evaluate your losing chances carefully. Once you know how near you are to a hopeless position you may appreciate what it takes to draw. Black is a pawn down and may lose another. Just a trade of queens would likely doom him. He realized he needs to take extreme measures. Even with just seconds left to reach the time control seven moves away, he saw tactical chances: White's queen is temporarily tied to the defense of g2, he lacks luft and Black has a passed pawn. With 1 ... c2! Black forced White to worry about 2 ... ♕b2/3 ... c1(♕). After White replied 2 ♖c1, Black came back with 2 ... ♖c8! and renewed the threat of 3 ... ♕b2! (3 ♖xc2?? ♕a1+). White's response, 3 ♕xe6, attacked the rook. But Black had 3 ... ♖d8! with a threat of 4 ... ♖d1+. White's lack of luft cost him (4 ♖xc2?? ♖d1 mate.) He played 4 ♕b3 but Black found 4 ... ♖d2!. Black was a move away (... ♕d4, ... ♕f6) from serious winning threats. White had no way of untangling himself so he forced a draw with checks at b8 and b2. Black's position became easier to play - even in bad time pressure - once he realized how desperate he had to be. A less clear situation arises when your position is not obviously worse but vulnerable to a bad trend: Some computers evaluate this position as more or less equal. They don't appreciate how useless the bishop would be when Black begins to push his f-pawn. Only when forced to examine 1 ... f5! followed by ... ♕e7/ ... f4 will those programs recognize that Black can make progress. White correctly understood how bad things could get and took the desperate- looking step of 1 ♖xe5! dxe5 2 d6. The White pawn cannot be prevented from reaching d7. On that protected square it will tie at least one Black piece to d8 and free the White queen to raid pawns. Now it was Black who was forced to evaluate his chances. He recognized his winning prospects had evaporated and went for the perpetual check of 2 ... ♕d8 3 ♕d5 ♕g5! and 4 ♕xc5 ♕c1+ 5 ♔h2 ♕f4+ 6 ♔g1 ♕c1+. What it Takes to Lose When queens are traded off the defender can almost always make a more accurate appraisal of his chances than before. That will tell him whether he can rely on passive defense or must seek counterplay, whether he should trade pawns or can await events, and so on. Appraising the position is easier if you know what it takes to lose - or to win. White is lost because his rook is not far enough away to check the Black king when it tries to shepherd the winning f-pawn. For example, 1 ... ♔e3! threatens 2 ... ♖-moves and 3 ... f1(♕). Then 2 ♖e5+ can be met by 2 ... ♔f4!. This attack on the rook gains time for Black and he can win in various ways, such as 3 ♖e8 ♖e1 4 ♖f8+ ♔g3. Yet in the game Black only drew because he didn't know what it takes to win. He played 1 ... ♔xc4??. White recovered with 2 ♖f8!. The rook is then in splendid position to check the king away if it tried to promote the f-pawn (2 ... ♔d4 3 ♖d8+ ♔e5 4 ♖e8+ ♔d6 5 ♖f8 etc.). Black's only winning try lies in advancing the a-pawn. But after ♔a2 White can just pass with his rook or check. For example, 2 ... a5 3 ♖f7 a4 4 ♖f8! a3+ 5 ♔a2 (not 5 ♔xa3?? ♖a1+ or 5 ♔c2?? a2) ♔d3 6 ♖d8+ ♔e3 7 ♖e8+ ♔d2 8 ♖d8+ ♔c2 9 ♖f8 is one of many drawing lines. The defender is typically torn between impulses. The first is to play actively: "I want to force a draw. I don't want to give him a chance to find a way to win." The second is passive: "Come and get me. My position is solid. I'm not going to do something rash that might lose." Both are valid attitudes. In most cases, one is better than the other. In some cases, much better. This kind of position rarely occurs in master games - because masters would have agreed to a draw earlier. The reason is that if Black allows a trade of pawns, the result is likely to reach the famously drawn 'Philidor position.' And if he doesn't trade pawns he has no way to make progress. White doesn't have to do anything but 'pass' with his rook along the fourth rank to stop ... ♔g4. For example, 1 ♖b4 ♖a6 2 ♖c4 ♖a3+ 3 ♔g2 ♔h5 and now 4 ♖h4+ ♔g5 5 ♖b4 ♔f6 6 ♖b6+ ♔g5 7 ♖b4. Or 2 ... ♔f6 3 ♖b4 g5 4 ♖c4 g4+ 5 ♔f4 or even 5 ♔g2 ♔g5 6 ♖b4. Notice that in this last line White can also play actively and try to force a Philidor-like position with 3 g4. Play might go 3 ... ♖a3+ 4 ♔g2 ♔g5 5 gxf5 gxf5 and now 6 ♖b4 (pass) f4 7 ♖b8 ♔g4 8 ♖g8+ draws. But the last diagram could be a study: How can White lose in one (reasonable) move? He did it with 1 g4??. White wanted to force a draw, not wait for it. But active defense was a disastrous choice here. Black replied 1 ... ♖e4!. Now White faces a dismal choice between giving up a second pawn (2 ♖a8 ♖xg4) or the equally lost pawn ending, 2 ♖xe4 fxe4+ 3 ♔xe4 ♔xg4 or 3 ♔g3 e3 4 ♔f3 e2. Even in a battle between two of the world's top-20 players, a defender can make a losing choice between active and passive. If the pawn on f5 didn't exist White would have known that 1 ♖h3! is the road to Philidor. The c-pawn is irrelevant: 1 ... ♖xc5 2 ♔f1 (or 2 ♖a3, 2 ♖b3 etc.). If Black pushes his advanced pawn, 1 ... f3, the draw is obvious after 2 ♖h8! (2 ... ♔e3 3 ♖e8+ ♔f4 4 ♖g8 or 4 ♖e7 or 4 ♔f1). But White was hypnotized by that extra pawn and felt he had to play 1 ♖c8?. He was not lost - yet. But after 1 ... f3: White can still draw by putting his rook in a position to give check, with, 2 ♖a8, 2 ♖b8, 2 ♖d8, or to check immediately, 2 ♖e8+. But he blundered with 2 c6?? and resigned after 2 ... f2+ in view of 3 ♔f1 ♔f3 and ... ♖c1 mate. (In fact, 2 ♔f1 would also draw because then 2 ... f2 would alert White to play 2 ♖a8, 2 ♖b8 etc. The opposite side of the coin is shown by this. Passive play is fatal: Since Black only has one pawn, its elimination would, at worst, leave White to defend K+R-vs.-K+R+B. That is a difficult endgame but it is drawable with best play. Therefore White should get rid of the Black pawn by means of f2-f3 and g3- g4!. But White played 1 f4?, depriving himself of the pawn-trading option. Worse, it fixed his f- and -g-pawns on squares where they will ultimately be lost. White held out for a long time but the course of the game, after 1 ... ♖g8 2 ♖b7 ♔d6 3 ♔f2 ♗d7 4 ♖b6+ ♔e7 5 ♖g6 ♔f7 6 ♖d6 ♗e6 7 ♖a6 ♖xg7, was determined. New Defense in the Endgame If New Defense has given us any insight into the endgame it is an emphasis on - guess what? - counterplay. Here's how we look today at one of the great endings of the past. This endgame has found its way into dozens of textbooks. It was used as a model of how to make slow progress: 1 ♖c3 ♖a4 2 ♖d3 ♔e7 3 ♔g3 ♔e6 4 ♔f3 ♔d5 5 ♔e2 g5! (Not 5 ... ♖xd4? 6 ♔e3! when White's outside passed pawn allows him to draw the pawn ending). Black won after 6 ♖b3 f6 7 ♔e3 ♔c4 8 ♖d3 d5! 9 ♔e2 ♖a7 10 ♔d2 ♖e7! 11 ♖c3+ ♔xd4. Although White wrote a book about rook endgames his play is much worse than analysts have given him credit. As Russian GM Alexey Bezgodov put it, he played at about the level of an Expert. The real lesson of this game should be: Rook endings reward active play and punish passivity. Grab your hand whenever it tries to make a passive rook move. One way to start is 1 ♖a2, getting behind the passed pawn and threatening to tie up Black's rook after 2 a4, 3 a5, etc. Black should blockade, 1 ... ♖a4!. But after 2 ♔g3 White's king races to break the blockade, e.g. 2 ... ♔e7 3 ♔f3 ♔e6 4 ♔e4 and then 4 ... f5+ 5 ♔d3 ♔d5 6 ♔c3, for example. When Black captures on d4 he releases the a-pawn, e.g. 2 ... ♖xd4 3 a4!. Yes, Black can re-establish the blockade with 3 ... ♖b4 4 a5 ♖b7 5 a6 ♖a7. But then his pieces are too passive to make progress after 6 ♔f4 ♔e7 7 ♔e4 ♔e6 8 ♖a5!. White could also have drawn with another active use of the rook, 1 ♖c6! ♔e7 2 ♖c7+ ♔f6 3 ♖c6! ♔e6 4 ♖c7! and then 4 ... ♖xa3 5 d5+! (5 ... ♔xd5 6 ♖xf7) ♔f6 6 ♖d7! ♖a6 7 h4. Black has no way to make progress. Passing fails (7 ... ♖b6 8 ♔g3). And there's another way to draw with an active rook. After the game went 1 ♖c3? ♖a4 White could have played 2 ♔g3! ♖xd4 3 ♖c8+ ♔e7 4 ♖a8! and again he has made his rook active. Black will eventually have to trade his d-pawn for the White a-pawn, e.g. 4 ... ♖d3+ 5 f3 g5 6 a4 ♖a3 7 a5 d5 8 a5! d5 9 a6 d4 10 ♖a7+ ♔e6 11 ♖a8 d3 12 ♖d8!. There are still other ways to draw, after the game went 2 ♖d3? ♔e7. Even here active play is rewarded: 3 ♖e3+! ♔d7 4 ♖f3! f6 5 ♖g3! g5 6 h4!. Just keep the rook active! The Insufficient Extra Pawn A good defender knows a basic difference: If he is down two pawns, his position is almost certainly lost. But if he is down only one pawn, the outcome depends on other factors. Those factors are the identity of what other pieces are on the board, how many pawns are left and where they are. That sounds like a lot of information but it gets easier to sort out as you gain experience. For example, one extra pawn is usually not enough to win if it's a rook ending and all the pawns are on the kingside. In the next example, White has much better chances if he can trade rooks rather than knights. He should proceed slowly, such as with 1 g3 and 2 ♖c7 or 2 ♖c6. He should avoid the immediate 1 ♖c6 because 1 ... ♘e5! would force knights off the board. A key point is 2 ♖xf6 ♘xf3+ 3 ♖xf3?? allows 3 ... ♖e1 mate. That means 3 gxf3 would be forced. The pawn structure that results is highly drawish. Endgame books show many examples of R+f-pawn+h-pawn-versus-R that are drawn. In the game, White made it easier, with 1 g4?. Then 1 ... ♘e5! either wins the g- pawn or trades knights. White played 2 ♘xe5 ♖xe5. Black was able to swap a pair of pawns, 3 ♔g2 h5!, and a draw was agreed soon after 4 f4 ♖e2+ 5 ♔f3 ♖xh2 6 ♔g3 ♖a2 7 gxh5 ♖a5 8 ♔g4 ♔g7. White's king cannot advance and his lone rook cannot promote the passed h- pawn. After benefiting from active moves (1 ... ♘e5! and 3 ... h5!) Black can now rely on passive defense: He can just pass. In fact, Black could also draw with other passes such as 8 ... ♖b5. The more generic drawable endings you know, the greater your ability to steer out of a dubious middlegame. For example, even with more pawns on the kingside in a rook ending, the defender has excellent drawing chances when he's only one pawn down. Black's pinned pawn at c5 appears to be doomed by ♘d2-b3xc5. Black can't change the situation by 1 ... cxb4?? 2 ♖xc7 or 1 ... ♘b6 2 ♖xc5. However, he saved himself with a forcing sequence, beginning with 1 ... e4!. The worst would be behind him after 2 ♖xe4 cxb4!. The same goes for 2 ♘d4 ♘e5! because of 3 ♖xc5 ♖xc5 4 bxc5 ♘d3! and 5 ... ♖xc5. The crucial line goes 2 ♕xe4 ♕xe4 3 ♖xe4 cxb4. White can improve his winning chances with 4 ♖e8+! ♖xe8 5 ♖xc7. Material is still equal but White's powerfully placed rook must win at least a pawn. If Black tries to swap off all the queenside pawns, say with 5 ... ♘f6 6 ♖xa7 ♖c8 White retains chances with 7 ♔f1. Even worse is trading rooks, with 5 ... ♘b6 6 ♖xa7 ♖a8? because 7 ♖xa8+ ♘xa8 9 ♘d4 offers strong winning chances. Knight endgames are often much more winnable than rook endings with the same pawns. Black's saving grace was 5 ... ♘e5! and 6 ♘xe5 ♖xe5 7 ♖xa7 h5 6 h3 ♖b5. White cannot avoid ... b3/axb3/ ... ♖xb3. What will be left is K+R+4Ps-vs.- K+R+3Ps. But since all the pawns will be on the kingside and Black has no real weakness there, a draw is the most likely result. White tried to win with 7 g4 hxg4 8 hxg4 g6 9 ♔g2 b3 10 axb3 ♖xb3 11 g5 ♔g7 12 ♔g3. But he couldn't penetrate Black's position and a draw was eventually agreed, after 12 ... ♖c3 13 ♖e7 ♖a3 14 ♖d7 ♔f8 15 ♖c7 ♔g7 16 ♖c8 ♔h7 17 ♖c7 ♔g7 etc. Trick Positions There are a few unusual drawable positions that violate many endgame principles. A good defender knows them. Many of these involve rook pawns. Any good endgame textbook will show you the cases when K+Q-vs.-K+P is drawn if the defender has a rook pawn (or a bishop pawn) that is on the seventh and protected by a king. You can also find the notorious case of K+B+RP-vs.-K which is a draw when the defender's king can reach the queening square and that square cannot be attacked by the bishop. In addition, K+R+P-vs.-K+R may be drawn when it's a rook pawn - but quite lost in similar positions with any other pawn. This is confusing to many amateurs because it is generally bad for the defender to be facing a RP in cases of K+N+P- vs.-K+N or K+B+P-vs.-K+same colored B. In the simplest endgame of all, with just kings and a pawn (or two), the rook pawn often saves the defender. This looks resignable because White will win the a-pawn. For example, 1 ... ♔d4 can be met by 2 ♔d6 ♔c3 3 ♔c5 ♔b2 and now 4 a4! ♔xb3 5 ♔b5 ♔c3 6 ♔xa5. Black's king cannot get back in time to stop promotion, It gets shouldered out by the enemy king (6 ... ♔c4 7 ♔b6 ♔d5 8 a5 ♔d6 9 a6). A better try is 1 ... ♔d5 but White reaches the same positions after 2 a4. Yet Black drew with 1 ... a4!. The main point is 2 bxa4 ♔d5 3 ♔d7 ♔c5 and ... ♔b4xa4 also dooms the a2-pawn. A secondary point is that after White found 2 b4! ... ... Black replied 2 ... a3!. He will either win the b-pawn (3 b5 ♔d5) or the a-pawn (3 ♔d7 ♔d4 4 b5 ♔c3 5 b6 ♔b2 6 b7 ♔xa2). Special Cases: Bishops of Opposite Color There are also a few other special situations a defender should know. Even if there are pawns on both wings, you can often draw one pawn or even two pawns down when there are bishops of opposite color. Leonhardt - Teichmann, Karlsbad 1907
1 e4 e5 2 ♘f3 ♘c6 3 ♘c3 ♘f6 4 ♗b5 ♗b4 5 O-O O-O 6 d3 d6 7 ♗g5 ♘e7 8 ♗xf6
gxf6 9 ♘h4 c6 10 ♗c4 b5 11 ♗b3 ♗c5? 12 ♕h5 ♔g7 13 ♘e2 ♘g6 Black dodged disaster because 14 ♘g3 can be met by 14 ... ♘xh4 15 ♕xh4 f5! 16 ♘h5+ ♔h6! and his king is remarkably safe. Play went 14 ♘f5+ ♗xf5 15 exf5 ♘f4! 16 ♘xf4 exf4 17 ♕g4+ ♔h8 18 ♕xf4. Despite White's extra pawn and Black's doubled pawns, a draw is the most likely result simply because of the bishops. Black made a swap of all four rooks virtually inevitable after he took control of the e-file, 18 ... d5 19 d4 ♗d6 20 ♕h4 ♖e8!. Then came 21 ♖ae1 ♖xe1 22 ♖xe1 ♔g7 23 ♕g4+ ♔h8 24 ♕h4 ♔g7 25 c3 ♕d7 26 ♗c2 ♖e8 27 ♖e3 ♖xe3 28 fxe3. White retains remote winning chances if he can play an effective e3-e4. But Black snuffed them out with 28 ... b4 so that if 29 e4 bxc3 30 bxc3 dxe4 and ... c5. The game saw 29 cxb4 ♕b7!, which is better than 29 ... ♗xb4 30 ♕g3+ ♔h6 because it offers counterplay against White's queenside pawns. After 30 a3 a5! White didn't like the looks of 31 bxa5 ♕xb2, so a draw was agreed after 31 ♕g4+ ♔f8 32 ♕h4 ♔g7 33 ♕g4+ ♔f8 34 ♕h4 ♔g7. Fortress Good defenders try to trade pawns when they are behind in material because it reduces the chances of the opponent queening or of winning another pawn. But eliminating enemy pawns is crucial to another drawing technique - the creation of an impregnable fortress. In this position from a candidates match, White seems to have excellent chances of making his extra pawn count because it's a passed queenside pawn. After a routine move of the attacked rook, 1 ... ♖c8, White can secure his king, 2 g3, and prepare to increase pressure with ♕d5/♖b7. Black has no tactical tricks since 1 ... ♕e8, hoping for 2 ♘xc4? ♕xb5 with a draw, is foiled by 2 ♘d7! and 2 ... ♖c7 3 ♖d5. There is one other trap in the position and Black eagerly fell into it, 1 ... ♖xb4! 2 ♘c6, and then 2 ... ♖xb5 3 ♘xe7+ ♗xe7. White's material edge had grown but his winning chances are greatly diminished. He began the final stage with 4 ♕d7 ♖b1+ 5 ♔h2 ♗f8. To win White will need a passed pawn or the penetration of his king - and possibly both. His difficulties are illustrated by 6 g4 ♖b2 7 ♔g3 g5! with a likely draw. The game went 6 f4 h5 7 f5 ♖b6! 8 e4 g6! and the outlines of a fortress were becoming apparent. After 9 fxg6 ♖xg6 Black could shuttle his rook between g6 and e6 forever and draw. White made a valiant effort, 9 ♕d8 ♖d6 10 ♕g5 ♗g7 11 ♕e7 ♗f8 12 ♕g5 ♗g7 13 ♕f4 ♖a6 14 ♕b8+ ♗f8 15 ♕b5 ♖f6 and then 16 ♔h3 gxf5 17 exf5 ♖h6 18 g3. White's king can get as far as g5 but no further because of the Black rook. Play went 18 ... ♖h7 19 ♕e2 ♖h6 20 ♔h4 ♖h7 21 ♕e8 ♖h6 22 ♔g5 ♖h7 23 ♕c8 ♔g7 24 ♕d8 ♔g8. White made one last try, advancing his f-pawn: 25 ♔f4 ♖h6 26 ♔e5 ♔g7 27 ♕g5+ ♔h7 28 f6 and then 28 ... ♔h8 29 ♔e4 ♔h7 30 ♔d5 ♔h8. If the king heads for e8 with 31 ♔c6 Black has 31 ... ♖g6! 32 ♕xh5+ ♔g8 and he wins the f6-pawn (33 ♕e5 ♗g7!) or draws by repetition. White saw the light and agreed to a draw after 31 ♕e5 ♔g8 32 ♔e4 ♖g6 33 ♔f5 ♖g4 34 ♕e3. Simple techniques like trading pawns and obtaining bishops of opposite color and more elaborate ones such as fortress are powerful aids to the defender. But the most important element in drawing an ending is knowing what it takes to lose and when active defense is a must. And now for our final set of quiz positions. Quiz Black has all the winning chances. Evaluate 1 ♘d2. Black can draw easily. How? White's pawn is the more dangerous one. What can Black do? White appears to be a move or two (g2-g3, ♖d1-d7) away from gaining a second extra pawn and a textbook win. How does Black stop him? White's rook is tied to the a-file to prevent queening. But she envisions an iron- clad drawing position. Which? Solutions
1. Black can attack h2 by means of ... ♖h5 (after ... ♗g4, for example). If White
defends with h2-h3, Black would win with ... ♗xh3. This explains 1 ♕g1!, an extreme example of the principle of economy. Yes, White is using his best piece to defend just one square. But this is less of a concession than h2-h3. White's advantage emerged after 1 ... ♗g4 2 ♖a5 ♗e2 3 ♘b3 ♗b4 4 ♖a4 ♖b8 5 ♘e5 ♖bb5 6 ♘xc6, White would also have had the edge after 1 ... ♗xc5 2 dxc5 ♖xc5 3 ♘d6.
2. Giving up castling, 1 ... ♖g8 or 1 ... ♔f8, is too great a concession. But 1 ... g6 2
♗b5! is a problem (2 ... ♖d8 3 O-O and ♗c6 at some point). Best is 1 ... O-O even though it costs the Exchange, 2 ♗h6 g6 3 ♗xf8 ♗xf8. By changing the nature Black has strong counterchances such as 4 ♗b5 ♗h6 or, as the game went, 4 ♗e2? ♗h6 5 ♖c2 ♘f4 6 O-O ♗xg2, with advantage.
3. Black looked at 1 ... g6 2 ♘f6+ and considered it lost (2 ... ♗xf6 3 ♕xf6 cxb2 4
♗h6 ♕xf1+ 5 ♕xf1 and ♕b1xb2; 2 ... ♔h8 3 ♖h4 h5 4 ♖xh5+; 2 ... ♔g7 3 ♗h6+). "Desperation is in order," he wrote about the move he played, 1 ... f5!?. After 2 ♖xg7+ ♔h8 he would still lose after 3 ♕g3!, threatening ♖xh7+ and ♕g7 mate. For instance, 3 ... ♗f6 4 ♖xf5 cxb2 5 ♖xh7+! ♔xh7 6 ♘xf6+ ♖xf6 7 ♖h5+. But White found what looks like a simpler win, 3 ♕h3. The threat is 4 ♖xh7+! ♔xh7 5 ♘f6+ ♔g7 6 ♕h7+ ♔xf6 7 ♖xf5 mate. But Black had time for 3 ... ♕xf1+! 4 ♔xf1 cxb2. Without a rook at f1, the threat of queening prompted White to force a perpetual check, 5 ♖xh7+ ♔xh7 6 ♘f4+ ♔g8 7 ♕g3+ ♔h7 8 ♕g6+ ♔h8 9 ♕h6+.
4. The least expensive defense against the threat is 1 ... ♖e8!. Then on 2 d6 he
replied 2 ... ♘d5. Black has an edge after 3 ♕d3 ♕b6 (4 ♘bd2 h6 5 ♗h4 ♘f4! 6 ♕f1 ♖ac8) or, as the game went, 3 ♗xd8 ♖xe2. Better for White was 2 ♖e1 since that forces the move Black has been trying to avoid, 2 ... f6. Black would be better after 3 d6 ♘d5.
5. Black wins after 1 ♗xg5? fxg5 2 ♘xg5+ ♔f6! - a move that defends and
attacks. For example, 3 ♕f3+ ♔xg5!. White played 3 ♖xe6+, hoping for 3 ... ♖xe6 4 ♕f3+. But he resigned after 3 ... ♔xg5! 4 ♕f3 ♖c1+ 5 ♔g2 ♘h4+ 6 ♔g3 ♖g1+.
6. Black is as entitled to tactics as White. He won a pawn with 1 ... ♗xg4!, based
on 2 ♗xf6 ♗xf3 and 2 ♗xg4 ♘xg4 3 ♕xg4 ♗xd4+ (or 3 ♗xg7 ♕h5!, threatening mate on h2).
7. The position was roughly balanced before 1 f4?. That's an unjustified
attacking move that Black began to punish with 1 ... ♗d5!. White's pawns would be over-extended after 2 e4 ♖e8 3 ♖e3 ♗b7. So he maneuvered his heavy pieces, 2 ♖d2 ♗e4! 3 ♕f2, while Black made progress with 3 ... ♖d7 4 ♕g3 ♖e8 5 ♕f2 ♗d3!. The e-pawn is falling (6 ♖c1 ♖de7 7 ♖e1? ♕xd4! 8 exd4 ♖xe1+) and Black won after 6 ♖c1 ♖de7 7 ♘f3 ♖xe3.
8. Black violated the rule against making new weaknesses with 1 ... f5!. White
would get nowhere from 2 ♕g3+ ♔h7 3 ♖fd1 ♘c5. He penetrated the eighth rank, 2 ♖fd1 ♘c5 3 ♖d8. But the improved communication of Black pieces stopped him, 3 ... ♕f7 4 ♖xe8 ♕xe8, and Black took over the initiative after 5 ♗d4 ♘e4 6 f3 e5! 7 fxe4 exd4.
9. Yes, 1 ... f6! is much better. It is based tactically on 2 ♘xf6+ ♗xf6 3 ♕xf6 ♖f8 4
♕-moves ♖xf3! since 5 gxf3 ♕xf3 leads to mate. It is based positionally on the superior pawn structure that Black will have after ... e5. He won after 2 ♗h6 O-O 3 ♗xg7 ♔xg7 4 ♖fe1 e5 5 b4 ♖ad8 and ... ♘e6-f4.
10. Black can't safely castle kingside. Delaying a decision about his king (1 ... e6
2 O-O ♗d6 3 ♖ae1) doesn't help.
It's time for an ugly move, 1 ... ♔d8!, to get the king to safety at b8. One possible line would run 2 O-O ♔c7 3 ♔h1 ♔b8 and Black stands well.
11. Endgames should favor Black's superior pawns. He won after 1 ... h5! 2 ♖af1
♕g4!.
12. The first two options allow the dangerous h-file to be opened. Black may be
able to escape with his material - computers tend to like 1 ... gxh5 2 ♖xh5 ♗f6 and 3 ♖g1 ♕d7. But for humans, the safest policy is 1 ... g5! since after 2 ♗xg5 Black is still a pawn ahead and the pawn at h5 is usefully 'dead.' The game went 2 ... ♕d6 3 ♖h4. Then 3 ... g3 4 ♖g4 ♔h8 is wrong because 5 ♗xe7! wins. Black gave back a second extra pawn with 3 ... ♘f6! 4 ♗xf6 ♕xf6 5 ♖xg4+ ♔h8 and had a strong attack (6 ♖g6? ♗f4! wins).
13. Black is safe after 1 ♗xh6 gxh6 2 ♖xh6 ♗f6. But in the longer run, g7 and
h6 can become targets after ♘g3-f5. Black made a wise decision to eliminate White's good bishop with 1 ... ♗g5!. Black stood well after 2 ♗xg5 ♕xg5 3 ♖h5 ♕g6 with ideas such as ... ♘f6-h7-g5. The weakest target on the board then is at f3. He got the edge following 4 ♘g3 ♘f6 5 ♘f5 ♗xf5 6 exf5? ♕e8 with the idea of ... e4. White's 7 ♖xh6? sacrifice was unsound and lost after 7 ... gxh6 8 ♖xh6 e4 9 ♕e3 ♕e5+ 10 ♔c1 ♖a1.
14. He can reinforce his king position with 1 ... ♖f8! followed by ... ♘e8 and ...
f6. This works because 2 ♘h5 ♘e8 3 ♗xg7 fails to 3 ... ♕g5!, threatening mate on g2 as well as ... ♘xg7. Black was better when a draw was agreed, after 3 ♖e3 f6 4 ♗d4.
15. No, Black seized the edge with 1 ... e5! 2 dxe5 ♗f5. He had good winning
chances after 3 ♖e2 ♗g4! 4 ♖ae1 ♗xf3 5 gxf3 ♖xe5.
16. He should flee. After 1 ... ♔f7! 2 g4 ♔e8! 3 ♖h2 ♔d7 Black was secure.
White's king could have been the more vulnerable one after 4 ♘g3 ♕f7 5 gxf5 gxf5 6 ♖h5 ♖g8. In the game White preferred 5 ♔g2 fxg4 6 ♗e3 and Black seized the initiative after 6 ... ♖h8.
17. With 1 ... ♗c8! Black reinforces the kingside with a piece that wasn't doing
much on b7. He looks at 2 ... ♗xf5 and is able to meet 2 ♗xe4 dxe4 3 ♖h3 h6 4 ♘xh6+ gxh6 5 ♕xh6 with 5 ... ♗xh3!. (In the game White played 5 ♖g3+ ♔h7 6 ♖xf7+ ♖xf7 7 ♕xf7+ ♔h8 and lost.) On the other hand, 1 ... g6 2 ♕h6! gxf5 3 ♗xe4 dxe4 would have walked into 4 ♖h3 ♕g7 5 ♖g3.
18. Once White's strong bishop is traded off and his d-pawn is block aded, his
weakened king position and the pawn at b2 will matter most. The game went 1 ... ♕b6 2 ♖d2 ♗f8! 3 ♖2e2 ♗d6! 4 ♕c1 g5! 5 ♗xd6 ♕xd6. White's d-pawn had gone from a strength to a weakness. His best chance for survival lay on the kingside but Black had the better chances there and won after 6 ♖f1 ♖f8 7 ♖2f2 ♗e8! 8 ♘f3 ♖g7 9 ♕d2 h5!.
19. White would have a strong attack after 1 ... fxg6 2 hxg6 hxg6 3 ♖dg1 or 2 ... h6
3 ♗xh6! gxh6 4 ♕xh6 (4 ... ♗f6 5 g7! ♗xg7 6 ♖dg1 and wins). With 1 ... ♗f6! and 2 ♖dg1 ♗a8 Black safeguarded g7 and began opera tions on the b-file. After 3 ♗g5 ♗e5 4 gxh7+ he avoided the dead pawn (4 ... ♔h8? 5 h6 g6 6 f4!) with 4 ... ♔xh7. Black can keep lines closed after 5 h6 g6 6 f4 ♗h8 (7 f5 ♘xe4). White tried to block the b-file with 5 ♘b3? and overlooked 5 ... ♘xc2! (6 ♕xc2 ♖xb3: 6 ♘xc5 ♘a3+! 7 ♔a2 ♕xc5 8 ♘a4 ♘c2! 9 ♔b1 ♕a3 and wins).
20. Black should realize that White has an attack brewing with g2-g4-g5.
Moreover, he should see that 1 ... dxc4? 2 ♘xc4 favors White's space and development edge. And he should understand that counterplay with 1 ... b5! is good. His attack would come first after 2 cxb5?! axb5 3 ♗xb5 ♗d7 or 3 ♘xb5 e5 4 ♗g3 ♗a6 and 4 ♕xc6 ♗d7 5 ♕c2 ♗xb5. In the game White was worse after 2 cxd5 exd5 3 g4 ♗e6.
21. In the center, with 1 ... f6! since 2 fxe6? fxe5 would give Black fine play. The
game went 2 exf6 ♗xf6 3 fxe6 d4! and Black had ample compensation. A draw resulted after 4 e7 ♕xe7 5 ♗xd4 ♗xd4 6 ♕xd4 ♕g5.
22. With the counterattack 1 ... ♕e7! 2 gxh6 ♕h4! Black has at least perpetual
check, e.g. 3 hxg7 ♗h3+ 4 ♔g1 ♕g3+ or 3 ♗xd5 ♗h3+ 4 ♔g1 ♕g3+ 5 ♗g2 ♗xg2 6 ♕xg2 ♕e1+.
23. With 1 ... ♖xd3! 2 ♖xd3 ♗e4 Black sharply reduced White's attacking
chances (3 ♖g3 ♕xc4 4 ♗xg7? ♗f5! 5 ♕h5 ♗xg7 6 ♕xh6 ♗g6). His loss of the Exchange was offset by winning a pawn, 3 ♖d2 ♕xc4. White set some traps and offered a second pawn, 4 ♖fd1 ♗d5 (else ♖d8!) 5 h3 f5 (not 5 ... ♕xa2 6 ♖xd5!) 6 ♕g6 ♕xf4. The end-result was a hard-fought draw after 7 ♖xd5! ♕e3+ 8 ♔h1 exd5 9 ♕xf5 ♖d8 10 ♖xd5 ♕c1+ 11 ♔h2 ♗d6+ 12 ♗e5 ♗c7!.
24. The less risky alternative to 1 h4 ♘xh4 2 gxh4 g3+ 3 ♔g1 ♕xh4 is 1 ♖xf5!
♗xf5 2 h4!. The attack is over and White pressure on the f-file and her great knight offers solid compensation. After 2 ... ♔b8 3 ♕e3 ♗c8 4 ♖f1 she can continue with ♖f4 and ♕f2 to tie Black to the f7-pawn while she prepares to attack with b2-b3 and c3-c4. Black decided to minimize her losing chances by trading rooks with 4 ... f6 5 ♖xf6 ♖gf8 6 ♖xf8 ♕xf8. But White was at least equal and following 7 ♘d3 ♕f5 8 ♘f4 ♕c2 she should have played to win with 9 e6! (9 ... ♕f5 10 b3! and c3-c4) rather than head for the draw as she did (9 ♕e2 ♕xe2 10 ♘xe2).
25. It's tempting to try 1 ... gxf5 2 ♕xf5 and defend against ♕xh7 mate with 2 ...
♘f6. Then 3 ♗g5 ♖e8! allows Black to escape and win. But this overlooks 3 ♗h6!, which threatens 4 ♕g5+ and ♕g7 mate. Then on 3 ... ♔h8 White has 4 ♗g5! with mate to follow ♗xf6+. Even if you didn't see all that, 1 ... ♗g5! was a good positional choice. Black got the edge after 2 ♕g4 ♗xc1 3 ♖axc1 ♘g7! (not 3 ... ♘xb2 4 ♘e7+) 4 ♘xg7 ♔xg7 and won the good-♘-vs.-bad-♗ position.
26. Black deserves more than perpetual check (1 ... ♔xg7 2 ♕g5+ ♔h8 3 ♕f6+).
But he shouldn't allow 2 ♗f6 or 2 ♕g5. The right response to the sacrifice was 1 ... ♕f5!. Black had the edge af ter 2 ♗h6 ♖e2! 3 ♕g5+ ♕xg5 4 ♗xg5 ♗e5! 5 ♖d1 ♗xb2 (or 5 ♖b1? ♗d4+!) and won the endgame.
27. Not 1 ... h6?, which loses to 2 ♗xh6! (2 ... ♘g8 3 ♗g5+ ♘h6 allows 4 f6!).
Once again the saving move is 1 ... ♔g8!. White grabbed the pawn, 2 ♗xf6 ♗xf6 3 ♕xh7+ ♔f8, lost the initiative after 4 ♕h8+ ♔e7 5 ♕h5 ♖d7 6 ♖e1+ ♔d8 7 ♖xe8+ ♔xe8 8 ♕h8+? ♔e7, and allowed Black a winning counterattack following 9 ♘d1 d4 10 ♕g8 ♕e5. There were better ways to play after 2 ♗xf6 but White might have been content to play 2 ♖g3, threatening ♗xf6. Then Black has nothing better than 2 ... ♔h8 - after which 3 ♖h3! ♔g8! leads to a draw by repetition (Not 2 ... ♔f8? 3 ♗h6!).
28. The position would be tactically dangerous if ♘xe7 or e6xf7 deliv ered check.
Black's 1 ... ♔h8! eliminated the checks. It had an additional benefit of threatening to trap the White bishop with ... ♘g8 and ... f6. For example, 2 ♖ad1 ♘g8! 3 ♘xe7 ♘xe7 4 ♗xe7 f6!. White replied 2 e5 dxe5 3 fxe5 but 3 ... ♘xd5! 4 ♘xd5 ♕xc6 was good. Now 5 ♘xe7?? is not check and allows 5 ... ♕xg2 mate. Play went 5 e6! because 5 ... fxe6 6 ♘f4! would have exploited the king's position at h8. Black played 5 ... ♘e5! and won after 6 ♖xe5? ♗xe5 7 exf7 (not check!) ♖f8 8 h3? ♖xf7 9 ♘f4 ♖xf4!. Afterwards it was pointed out that 6 ♗h6! was the right way: 6 ... f6 7 ♖xe5! fxe5 8 ♖f1 threatens ♖f7 (8 ... ♖f8? 9 ♗xg7+ ♔xg7 10 ♕xe7+). Black's best is the equality of 8 ... ♕xe6 9 ♘c7.
29. With 1 ... h5! Black halted White's kingside plans and created the prospect of
... ♘g4. Play went 2 ♔b1 ♘b6! 3 ♘d2? ♘g4! 4 ♗xe7 ♕xe7 and after 5 ♘f1 ♖xc3! 6 bxc3 d5 7 ♕e2 O-O Black had a winning attack.
30. Why give White chances when you can force at least a draw with 1 ... ♖c2! (2
♘xc2? ♗xc2+ and 3 ... ♕xe3). The game went 2 b3 ♗xb3! 3 axb3 ♖c3 with a draw, in view of 4 hxg7 ♖xb3+ 5 ♘xb3 ♕xb3+ 6 ♔a1! ♕a3+ (not 6 ♔c1? ♖c8+ 7 ♔d2 ♖c2+ and wins).
31. Both the overprotective 1 ... g6 and 1 ... ♕f7 2 ♕h3 ♗g6 3 ♕g4 ♔g8 (as Black
played) are good. But 1 ... ♗g6? just drops a piece (2 ♕xg6). And 1 ... ♗f7? is also bad because it would give Tal a chance to be Tal. He would start with 2 ♖xh7+ ♔xh7 3 ♖h1+ ♔g8 4 ♘g4!, which threatens ♘h6+. After 4 ... ♗e7 5 ♕h3 White would threaten ♕h8 mate as well as ♘h6+/ ♕xd7. This could have been one of Tal's greatest combinations: 5 ... ♔f8 6 ♗h6! ♗g8 7 ♗xg7+! ♔xg7 8 ♖g1! ♔f8 9 ♕h6+ ♔f7 10 ♘e5+! and mates.
32. It's not perpetual check because 2 ... ♔h8 3 ♕f6+ ♔g8 4 ♘f5! forces Black
to give up his queen. Black defended well with 1 ... ♕g4! 2 ♗xf6 c5! 3 ♘c2 ♕g6!. He confused White's pieces with 4 ♖f1 ♗e4 5 ♘e3 ♖ae8 6 h3 ♖e6! 7 ♘g4 h5! and won.
33. He can steal the initiative with 1 ... ♖c8+! 2 ♘xc8 ♕c7+!. Then 3 ♔d2 would
be met by 3 ... ♕a5+! 4 ♔c1 ♖xc8+. White chose 3 ♔b1 but Black had the edge following 3 ... ♖xc8 4 ♖6f2 ♗e6 7 ♕h5 b5 and 8 ... ♕a5.
34. With 1 ... ♔f7? Black leaves his king in potential danger after 2 ♖xe5 (2 ...
♔g8 3 ♕h4 and ♗f6). Black escaped to the queenside with 1 ... ♔d6! 2 dxe5+ ♔c7 and won after 3 e6 ♖g8 4 ♗f4+ ♗d6 5 ♗xd6+ ♔xd6 6 ♖d1+ ♔c7 7 ♕f4+ ♔c8. No better is 2 ♕f4 ♖g8! 3 ♖xe5 ♔d7 4 ♕f5+ ♔c7.
35. The a-pawn will cost White a piece and result in an Exchange-up ending with
two pawns apiece on the kingside. The knight should be left at bl so it can be sacrificed on a3 after ... a3. But in the game White lost after 1 ♘d2? ♖b2 2 ♔e3 a3 3 ♗f7 (otherwise the a-pawn queens) a2 4 ♗xa2 ♖xa2 5 ♘f3 ♔g8 6 ♔f4 ♖a4+ 7 ♔f5 ♖a3. The bishop defends better than the short-range knight because it can 'pass' and avoid zugzwang. The rest was 8 ♔f4 ♔f7 9 h5 ♔e6 10 g4 ♖a4+ 11 ♔g3 ♖b4 12 ♘h4 ♔e5 13 ♘f5 ♖b7 (zugzwang) 14 ♔h4 ♔f4 15 ♘d4 ♖b4 16 ♘e2+ ♔f3 17 ♘g1+ ♔g2! 18 ♘e2 ♖b1 19 ♘g3 ♖a1 and White resigned be cause a knight move allows 20 ... ♖h1 mate.
36. With 1 ... h3! and 2 gxh3 ♔e6 he reaches the queening square area and draws.
No better is 2 g4 ♔e6 3 ♔g3 ♔f6 4 ♔xh3 ♔g6, a book draw.
37. Black played 1 ... ♖g6+? 2 e6 and was close to losing. She completed the
collapse with 2 ... ♖g2 3 ♖xf3 ♖d2+ 4 ♔e7 and resigned when she realized the Lucena position was near. Black can draw with 1 ... f2! 2 ♖xf2 ♖a3! and giving checks on the a-file (or 3 ♖c2 ♖a6+ 4 ♖c6 ♖a8). Even after 1 ... ♖g6+? 2 e6 ♖g2 3 ♖xf3 she could have drawn with 3 ... ♖a2! 4 e7 ♖a6+!.
38. The active 1 ... ♖d8! ensures he can double rooks on the seventh rank. That
alone is not enough to draw, as 2 ♖xa7 ♖dd2 3 ♖a3 showed. Now 3 ... ♖xf2 regains a pawn but enables White, with 4 ♖g3+ ♔f8 5 a3! to keep the more valuable a-pawn. A much clearer path to a draw was 3 ... ♔f8!. White had nothing better than 4 ♖f1 ♖xa2 5 ♖xa2 ♖xa2, reaching a 3-to-2 pawn situation on the kingside. A draw was agreed soon after 6 g3 ♔g7 7 ♔g2 ♖b2 8 ♖a1 ♖c2 9 g4 h5! 10 gxh5 ♖c5 and ... ♖xh5 (or 10 g5 ♖c4 11 ♖a3 ♖g3+ 12 ♖g3 ♔g6).
39. With 1 e5+! ♔xe5 2 g4! White's secures a draw. Black's king cannot help
promote the a-pawn, 2 ... ♔d5 3 ♔g2 ♔c5 4 ♖a8 ♔b4 5 ♖a6 because 5 ... ♔b3 (threatening a rook move and queening) will be met by 6 ♖b6+. The other key point is that even if Black managed to achieve ... f5 and trade pawns, the result would look like this. If Black advances the g-pawn to g3 White will let it sit there (not ♔xg3?? because of ... ♖g1+ and queens). Black cannot make progress.
